Chapter 11 · Part V: Money and Prices in the Long Run

The Monetary System

What money actually is, how banks conjure it into existence, and why central banks hold the most concentrated economic power on the planet

On the morning of July 1, 1994, Brazilians woke up to a new currency. The day before, every price in the country was quoted in two units: cruzeiros reais (the old, hyperinflated currency) and URVs (Unidades Reais de Valor — "Real Units of Value," a stable index pegged to the U.S. dollar). On July 1, the URV simply became the new currency: the real. Every bank account, contract, salary, and price tag was converted at the prevailing exchange rate. Inflation, which had been running above 5,000% per year in 1993, fell to single digits within twelve months. It is the most successful currency stabilization in modern economic history — and one of the most important real-world experiments ever conducted on what money is.

What made the Brazilian Real Plan work was not money creation or destruction. The cruzeiro and the real had the same ultimate backing — the Brazilian government and central bank. What changed was the credibility of the currency as a measure of value, and that change rippled through every economic decision in the country. The Real Plan revealed something fundamental that this chapter will explore in depth: money is, at heart, a social institution. Its physical form (coins, paper, electronic balances) is almost incidental. What matters is the trust people place in it.

This chapter teaches you what money is — really is — and how the modern financial system creates it. By the end you will understand why a central bank can buy trillions of dollars of bonds without anyone "earning" the money first, why a bank panic can destroy half the money supply in weeks, and why Bitcoin enthusiasts and gold standard advocates are both wrestling with the same fundamental question of what gives a currency its value.

✦ Sharma's Take

Money is the most magical institution in economics, and almost every student arrives in this chapter with a wrong intuition about what it is. Most people believe that money is "backed" by something — gold reserves, government promises, the productive capacity of the economy. None of this is quite right. The U.S. dollar is not backed by gold (it hasn't been since 1971). It is not backed by an explicit government promise to do anything specific. The Federal Reserve doesn't promise to redeem dollars for any particular good or service.

What backs the dollar is something subtler and more powerful: everyone else's willingness to accept it in exchange for goods and services. As long as I can use a $20 bill to buy lunch tomorrow, the bill is worth $20 to me today. If everyone simultaneously decided dollars were worthless, dollars would be worthless. This sounds tautological. It almost is. But this circularity — money's value resting on a self-fulfilling expectation — is the most consequential coordination problem in human society. Most of the time it works beautifully. When it breaks (Weimar Germany 1923, Zimbabwe 2008, Brazil 1993, Venezuela 2018), it breaks catastrophically.

— A.S.

11.1 What Money Does: Three Functions

📘 Principles Level — Define money by what it does, not what it is

Economists don't define money by its physical form (coins, paper, deposits) — too narrow, since over the centuries money has taken hundreds of forms. They define it by what it does. Anything that performs all three of the following functions in an economy is, by economists' definition, money:

📚 The Three Functions of Money
Medium of exchange
A thing that sellers will routinely accept in exchange for goods or services. This is what eliminates the need for barter (the dreaded "double coincidence of wants" problem — finding someone who has what you want AND wants what you have). When everyone agrees to accept dollars, transactions become enormously easier.
Unit of account
A standard measure of value used to quote prices and record debts. When you ask "how much does this cost?", the answer is in a unit of account. Without a stable unit of account, businesses cannot compare options, write multi-year contracts, or do basic accounting. This was the function Brazil's URV was designed to restore.
Store of value
A thing that holds purchasing power over time. If you earn money today and want to spend it next month, you need a store of value to hold the wealth in the meantime. Money fails as a store of value during high inflation (the value erodes), which is why during hyperinflations people switch to dollars, gold, or real estate.
✦ Sharma's Take — Why hyperinflation happens "all at once"

Notice the relationship between the three functions. As long as something stays a usable medium of exchange and unit of account, modest losses in store-of-value function (a few percent inflation) are tolerable. But once high inflation erodes the store-of-value role enough that businesses lose faith in the local currency as a stable measure, they begin quoting prices in a foreign stable currency — typically U.S. dollars in Latin America, euros in Eastern Europe — even though the actual transaction is still settled in the local currency. At that point the unit-of-account function has collapsed: the local currency is no longer trusted as a yardstick of value, even though it is still being used to pay. With unit-of-account gone, contracts become impossible, planning falls apart, and people refuse to accept the local currency for transactions — destroying the medium-of-exchange function. The three functions are stable individually but mutually reinforcing. Once one starts to fail, the others follow within months. This is why high inflation rarely stays modest — it tips into crisis once a threshold is crossed.

The genius of the Brazilian Real Plan was recognizing this dynamic and engineering a controlled separation of the functions. The URV took over the unit-of-account role while the cruzeiro continued to handle medium-of-exchange. By stabilizing the unit-of-account function first, Brazil broke the inflation-inertia cycle without forcing an immediate currency replacement. Then, once everyone had been thinking in URV for four months, the conversion to the real currency was almost a formality.

— A.S.

11.2 Kinds of Money: From Cowrie Shells to Cryptocurrency

📘 Principles Level

Money has taken bewilderingly diverse forms over human history: cattle in ancient India, cowrie shells in West Africa and East Asia, copper rings in West Africa, large carved stones in Yap, salt blocks in Ethiopia, tobacco leaves in colonial Virginia, beaver pelts in colonial Canada, gold coins in classical Greece and Rome, paper notes in Tang-dynasty China, electronic balances today. What all of these have had in common is that society agreed on them as media of exchange. What distinguishes them is whether their physical form had value beyond the monetary function.

Kinds of money — distinguished by where their value comes from
TypeWhat it isExamples
Commodity money An object with intrinsic value used as money — has worth even if not used as currency Gold/silver coins, salt blocks, tobacco leaves, cattle. The commodity could always be melted down or consumed.
Commodity-backed money Tokens (paper, coins) that have no intrinsic value but are convertible on demand into a commodity Pre-1971 U.S. dollars (convertible to gold via central banks); 19th-century banknotes redeemable in silver.
Fiat money Tokens with no intrinsic value, no convertibility — value comes purely from social acceptance and government decree Modern U.S. dollars, euros, yen, pounds. "Fiat" is Latin for "let it be done" — government declares the token to be money.
Cryptocurrency Decentralized digital tokens whose supply is governed by software protocol; no central issuer, no intrinsic value Bitcoin, Ethereum. Mostly used as speculative store of value rather than medium of exchange. El Salvador adopted Bitcoin as legal tender in 2021 (results so far: limited everyday use).
⚠ Common Pitfall — "Fiat money is worthless without backing"

A common claim from gold-standard advocates and cryptocurrency enthusiasts is that fiat money is "worthless" because it isn't backed by anything physical. This is a misunderstanding of what backing means. Fiat money IS backed — but by something more powerful than gold reserves: by the credibility of the issuing institution and the network effect of universal acceptance.

Consider: throughout history, governments on the gold standard have repeatedly devalued their currencies by changing the gold conversion ratio whenever it suited them. Britain abandoned gold in 1931. The U.S. devalued gold backing in 1934 (forcing private gold turn-in at $20.67/oz, then revaluing to $35) and abandoned the link entirely in 1971. Pure commodity backing didn't prevent debasement — it just made the politics of debasement noisier.

Meanwhile, the U.S. dollar has held its value reasonably well over the past 50 years (modest inflation averaging ~3-4% per year) because the Federal Reserve has, on the whole, defended its mandate. The "backing" is the Fed's institutional credibility, the Constitution's structure, and Americans' continued willingness to accept dollars. That backing turned out to be more durable than the metallic kind.

11.3 Measuring the Money Supply: M1 and M2

📘 Principles Level — How much money exists?

Once we agree that "money" is whatever serves the three functions, an obvious question follows: how much of it is in the economy at any given time? The answer turns out to be surprisingly contested, because the line between "money" and "highly liquid asset that's almost as good as money" is fuzzy. The Federal Reserve publishes two main measures, each drawing the line in a different place.

📚 The U.S. Money Supply Aggregates
Currency
Physical bills and coins in circulation outside banks. About $2.4 trillion in early 2026 — roughly $7,000 per American on paper, although a substantial portion is held abroad as a store of value or in the underground economy.
Demand deposits
Checking-account balances available on demand for spending. About $5 trillion in early 2026.
M1
The narrow money supply. Currency + demand deposits + other liquid checkable deposits. About $18.5 trillion as of early 2026. This is "money" in the most usable sense — what you can spend immediately without converting anything.
M2
The broader money supply. M1 + savings deposits + small time deposits (under $100,000) + retail money-market mutual funds. About $22.4 trillion as of January 2026. This includes "near-money" — slightly less liquid balances that can be converted to spending money quickly.
Monetary base (M0 or "high-powered money")
Currency in circulation + reserves held by banks at the Federal Reserve. Around $5.6 trillion currently. This is the money the Fed directly creates and controls — the foundation upon which the banking system builds the broader M1 and M2.
✦ Sharma's Take — Why M2 has been the Fed's preferred measure

For decades, the Fed paid close attention to M2 as a leading indicator of inflation. The intuition came from the quantity theory of money (chapter 12): if M grows much faster than real output, prices should rise. From the late 1980s through the early 2000s, this relationship was reasonably reliable, and many central banks targeted M2 growth ranges directly.

Then around 2010, the relationship broke down. Following the Global Financial Crisis, the Fed expanded its balance sheet from about $900 billion to over $4 trillion through quantitative easing. The monetary base exploded. Yet inflation remained below target for most of the next decade. The reason is that the new reserves the Fed created mostly stayed parked at the Fed (banks held them as excess reserves earning interest from the Fed) rather than being multiplied into M2 through new loans. The transmission mechanism between the monetary base and the broader money supply broke down.

The Fed has subsequently de-emphasized M2 in favor of direct interest-rate policy and inflation targeting. But the M2 indicator did warn of trouble in 2021: M2 grew over 25% year-over-year due to the combination of QE and pandemic stimulus payments. Two years later, U.S. inflation peaked at 9.1%. The relationship is messy, but it's not dead.

— A.S.

11.4 Banks and the Creation of Money

📘 Principles Level — How a deposit becomes more deposits

Here is the most counterintuitive fact in introductory macroeconomics: banks create money. Not in the literal sense of printing bills (only the central bank can do that), but in the sense that the M1 and M2 money supply rises whenever a bank makes a new loan. The mechanism is so subtle that most people who use banks every day don't realize it's happening.

Imagine you deposit $1,000 of cash into your checking account at First National Bank. From your perspective, you still have $1,000 — it's in your checking account, available on demand. But the bank doesn't keep your $1,000 sitting in its vault. It keeps a small fraction (say 10%) as a reserve against possible withdrawals, and lends the other 90% — $900 — to a borrower. The borrower now has $900 to spend. You still have your $1,000 in the bank. The total spending power in the economy has just risen from $1,000 to $1,900.

The borrower then spends the $900 on, say, restaurant supplies. The restaurant deposits the $900 in its bank, which keeps $90 as reserve and lends out $810. Now there's $1,000 + $900 + $810 = $2,710 in spending power. The cycle continues. With a 10% reserve ratio, the eventual total of money created from your initial $1,000 deposit is approximately $10,000.

📚 Key Banking Terms
Reserve
The portion of deposits a bank keeps available rather than lending out. Held either as vault cash or as balances at the Federal Reserve.
Reserve ratio
The fraction of deposits a bank keeps as reserves. Historically set by the Fed (the required reserve ratio) but reduced to 0% in 2020. Today, banks hold reserves voluntarily, based on risk management and the interest the Fed pays on reserves.
Fractional reserve banking
The system in which banks keep only a fraction of deposits as reserves and lend out the rest. The basis of essentially all modern banking. Without it, banks couldn't perform their economic function of channeling saving into investment.
Bank run
A situation where many depositors simultaneously demand their money back. Because the bank keeps only fractional reserves, if too many depositors withdraw at once, the bank cannot meet demand — even if it is fundamentally solvent. Bank runs ended dozens of U.S. banks every year before 1933 and contributed enormously to the Great Depression. Mostly stopped after FDIC deposit insurance was created in 1933.
⚙ Equation Walkthrough — The Money Multiplier
m = 1 / R (so total money created from a $1 deposit ≈ $1 / R)
m
The money multiplier. Tells you how many dollars of M1 are created in the banking system from one new dollar of monetary base.
R
The reserve ratio — fraction of deposits that banks keep as reserves rather than lending out. R is between 0 and 1.
1 / R
The reciprocal of R. Comes from the geometric series: 1 + (1−R) + (1−R)² + ... = 1/R, which sums up the new deposits created at each round of lending.

Reading it in plain language: If banks keep 10% of deposits as reserves (R = 0.10), then each $1 of new monetary base eventually expands the M1 money supply by $10. If banks keep 20% (R = 0.20), the multiplier is $5. The smaller the reserve ratio, the more aggressive the expansion. The basic insight is that the same dollar can be the basis of many separate transactions, as long as not everyone tries to withdraw at once.

Why the simple formula understates the reality: the formula assumes (1) banks lend out exactly what they don't keep as reserves, (2) all loaned money is redeposited, (3) reserves come only from required-reserve regulation. In modern banking, banks may hold excess reserves beyond what's required, people may hold cash (which doesn't get redeposited), and banks may face other constraints. The actual money multiplier in the U.S. today is closer to 3-4 rather than the textbook's hypothetical 10, mainly because banks hold large excess reserves.

📐 Worked Example 11.1 — Tracing money creation
A central bank creates $100 of new monetary base by buying a Treasury bond from a private seller, who deposits the proceeds at Bank A. Banks, all of them, maintain a 10% reserve ratio and lend out the rest. Trace the first three rounds of deposit creation, then estimate the total final M1 expansion.
1
Round 1. Bank A receives $100 deposit. Reserves = $10, loan = $90. M1 expanded so far: $100 (the original deposit) + $90 (the new loan, which the borrower can spend) = $190
2
Round 2. The $90 loan is spent and redeposited at Bank B. Reserves = $9, new loan = $81. M1 now: $100 + $90 + $81 = $271
3
Round 3. The $81 loan is redeposited at Bank C. Reserves = $8.10, new loan = $72.90. M1 now: $100 + $90 + $81 + $72.90 = $343.90
4
Pattern. Each round adds 0.9 times the previous round's loan. The geometric series sums to: $100 × (1 / 0.10) = $1,000 total deposits created.
The original $100 of monetary base ultimately supports $1,000 of M1 deposits. The monetary base rose by $100, but the broader money supply rose by approximately 10 times that. This is the textbook money multiplier in action. Important caveat: in practice, banks hold excess reserves and people hold cash, so the realized multiplier is much smaller than the simple formula predicts.

✏ Try This — A bank run reverses the process

Suppose in the same economy, depositors panic and withdraw $100 of cash from Bank A. Reserves at Bank A fall by $100. To restore its 10% reserve ratio, what must Bank A do? Estimate the total contraction of M1.

▸ Reveal answer
Bank A must call in $900 of loans to restore its reserve ratio (because losing $100 of reserves means it can't support $1,000 of deposits at 10% — it can only support $0). Those borrowers, when forced to repay, withdraw money from their banks, which then must call in their own loans, and so on. The total contraction is approximately $1,000 of M1 destroyed from a $100 cash withdrawal — the multiplier works in reverse. This is exactly what happened thousands of times during the 1929-33 banking panics, when about a third of U.S. banks failed and the M1 money supply contracted by approximately 25% — a major contributor to the Depression's severity.

11.5 The Federal Reserve: How Central Banks Control Money

📘 Principles Level — The most powerful institution in the U.S. economy

The Federal Reserve System (informally "the Fed") is the central bank of the United States. Created by Congress in 1913 in response to repeated banking panics, it has three core responsibilities:

  1. Conduct monetary policy — set short-term interest rates and influence the money supply to pursue the dual mandate of maximum employment and price stability (defined since 2012 as 2% PCE inflation).
  2. Supervise and regulate banks — set capital requirements, conduct stress tests, intervene in failing banks. After 2008, this role expanded significantly under the Dodd-Frank Act.
  3. Provide liquidity in crises — act as lender of last resort when banks face panic, lending against good collateral to prevent solvent banks from failing due to temporary cash shortages. This was the role for which central banks were originally invented.

The Fed has five tools to affect the money supply. The table below lists them in roughly the order they are used today — from primary daily tools at the top to deprecated or crisis-only tools at the bottom:

The Fed's monetary policy toolkit, listed by current importance
ToolHow it worksWhen used
Open-market operations The Fed buys (or sells) government securities in the open market. Buying creates new reserves; selling absorbs reserves. The primary, daily tool. The Fed uses it to keep the federal funds rate (the rate banks charge each other for overnight reserves) at its target.
Interest on reserves Since 2008, the Fed pays banks interest on the reserves they hold at the Fed. Raising this rate makes banks willing to hold more reserves rather than lending them out. The main lever for setting the floor on short-term rates in the modern (post-2008) "ample reserves" regime.
Discount window The Fed lends directly to banks at a stated rate, accepting collateral. Originally the main lender-of-last-resort mechanism. Less central today; used in crises (2008-09, March 2020, March 2023) to provide emergency liquidity to specific banks.
Reserve requirements Historically set by the Fed; banks had to hold a minimum fraction of deposits as reserves (10% on transaction accounts). Reduced to 0% in March 2020 and not restored. Largely deprecated as an active tool; banks today hold reserves based on their own risk management.
Quantitative easing (QE) Large-scale asset purchases beyond short-term Treasuries — including long-term bonds and mortgage-backed securities. Floods the banking system with reserves. Used in major crises when the federal funds rate is already at or near zero. Deployed in 2008-2014, 2020-2022.
⚠ Common Pitfall — "Money printing causes inflation immediately"

A widely repeated claim is that "central bank money printing always causes inflation." This is half-right and dangerously oversimplified. Money creation causes inflation only when the new money flows into actual spending. If new reserves sit at the Fed (as they did 2008-2019, when banks held trillions in excess reserves), they don't cause inflation because they aren't being spent on goods and services.

The Fed's balance sheet expanded from $900B to $4.5T between 2008 and 2014. CPI inflation over that period averaged 1.6% per year — below the Fed's 2% target. By contrast, the Fed's balance sheet plus pandemic fiscal stimulus from 2020-2021 caused M2 to surge over 25% YoY, AND the stimulus checks were spent rather than saved, AND supply chains were disrupted. Two years later, inflation peaked at 9.1%.

The lesson: money creation is necessary but not sufficient for inflation. What matters is whether the new money translates into spending on real goods and services. This insight will be formalized in chapter 12 (the quantity theory of money) and is critical for understanding modern monetary policy.

11.6 The Real Plan: How Brazil Defeated 5,000% Inflation Without Printing Less

1993–1994 Brazil Currency stabilization

The Most Successful Currency Stabilization in Modern History

By 1993, Brazil's monthly inflation rate routinely exceeded 40%. Annual inflation that year passed 5,000%. Brazilians shopping in supermarkets watched price-tag stickers being changed by store employees who walked the aisles all day with pricing guns. Salaries had to be paid weekly — even daily — because by month's end, monthly wages had lost half their purchasing power. Multiple previous stabilization plans — Cruzado (1986), Bresser (1987), Verão (1989), Collor (1990), Collor II (1991) — had all failed, often catastrophically. Brazil had earned the unwanted distinction of being the textbook case for chronic inflation that no government could break.

What broke the cycle was a remarkable insight from a small group of economists at PUC-Rio (Persio Arida, André Lara Resende, Edmar Bacha, and others). They diagnosed Brazilian inflation as having two distinct causes: (1) ongoing fiscal deficits and money creation (the standard story), but also (2) inflation inertia — the self-reinforcing dynamic where every economic agent raised prices preemptively because they expected everyone else to raise prices. Even if the government fixed the underlying fiscal problem, inertia would keep inflation going.

Their solution was to attack the unit-of-account function of money directly. On March 1, 1994, the government introduced the Unidade Real de Valor (URV) — Real Unit of Value — a non-monetary index whose value was pegged to roughly $1 USD. Crucially, the URV was a unit of account only: prices, wages, and contracts were quoted in URV, but actual payments were still made in old cruzeiros reais. The exchange rate between URV and cruzeiros was recalculated daily and published by the central bank.

For four months — March, April, May, June 1994 — Brazilians lived in a strange transitional economy. They saw prices in URV (stable), saw the same prices in cruzeiros (rising daily), and did all their thinking and contracting in URV. By the time most prices were quoted in URV, inflation expectations had effectively been re-anchored. Then, on July 1, 1994, the government simply made the URV the new currency, called the real (R$). Bank balances, contracts, and prices converted at the day's URV-cruzeiro rate. The old hyperinflated cruzeiro disappeared overnight.

The result was extraordinary. Annual inflation fell from over 5,000% in 1993 to about 22% in 1995, then to single digits within a few more years. The architects of the plan, Persio Arida and his colleagues, became national heroes. Finance Minister Fernando Henrique Cardoso, who had championed the plan, won the 1994 presidential election largely on its strength. Brazil has had its share of problems since — fiscal stress, recessions, political turmoil — but it has not returned to chronic inflation in the 30+ years since the plan.

What makes this case so important pedagogically: the Real Plan worked not by changing the monetary base or money creation rules, but by repairing the unit-of-account function of money. It is the cleanest real-world demonstration that money's value is fundamentally about expectations and credibility, not about the physical quantity of bills in circulation.

Stop and reflect: If money is fundamentally about expectations, what does that imply about the role of central bank credibility? Why do central banks fight so hard to maintain their independence from political pressure? What might happen to the U.S. dollar if markets came to believe the Fed was no longer committed to price stability? The Brazilian case suggests the answer should worry policymakers: once credibility goes, restoring it is enormously costly and uncertain.

11.7 The Money Multiplier in Action: Interactive Calculator

🎛 Interactive — Bank Money Creation Visualizer

Watch how a single deposit cascades through the banking system

What you're seeing: the bars show the new deposits created at each round of bank lending. The first round is your initial deposit; each subsequent round is the previous round multiplied by (1 − reserve ratio). The total of all rounds is the M1 expansion.

Try: set reserve ratio to 20%. Notice the multiplier falls to 5×. Then drop to 5% — multiplier rises to 20×. The lower the reserve ratio, the more aggressive the money creation. This is why the Fed historically used reserve requirements as a policy tool.

11.8 Why Central Bank Independence Matters

📘 Principles Level — The temptation to print money

Money creation is enormously tempting for governments. Politicians who control the printing press can fund wars, finance pet projects, redistribute wealth, or buy electoral support without raising taxes. The cost — inflation — falls on the broader public over time, often after the politicians have left office. This temporal mismatch is the classic time-inconsistency problem in monetary policy: governments that promise low inflation often find it irresistible to break the promise once the public has accepted the promise as credible.

The institutional response, developed throughout the 20th century, is central bank independence: structurally insulating the monetary authority from short-term political pressure. The Fed's chair serves a 4-year term that doesn't align with the presidential election cycle. The Fed's governors serve 14-year terms (one of the longest tenures in U.S. government). The Fed's budget comes from interest income on its own balance sheet, not from congressional appropriation. The European Central Bank goes further: any direct lending to governments is explicitly prohibited by treaty.

The empirical evidence is striking. Cross-country studies (Alesina & Summers, 1993; many followups) show a clear inverse relationship: countries with more independent central banks have lower average inflation, with no apparent cost in growth or unemployment. The U.S., Germany, Switzerland, and other countries with strong central bank independence have mostly avoided the inflationary spirals that have plagued Argentina, Turkey, Venezuela, and Zimbabwe — countries where the central bank ultimately answered to the political leadership.

🎮 MacroNations Connection — The Money Supply and Inflation

In MacroNations, your Money Supply Growth lever directly affects the broader money aggregates in the simulation. Beyond a critical threshold (roughly real GDP growth + 2% target inflation), additional money growth begins translating into inflation per the quantity theory framework (chapter 12). The Central Bank Independence parameter (a slow-moving institutional variable) determines how sticky inflation expectations are when the central bank promises stability.

The Brazil-style scenario can play out in MacroNations: a country with weak central bank independence that runs persistent fiscal deficits financed by money creation will slide into self-reinforcing inflation, with the cost of restoring credibility (in lost output) much higher than the cost of preventing the slide in the first place. Strategies that preserve central bank independence and credibility early generate massive long-run gains via the Stability pillar.

U.S. M2 Money Supply (Billions, Seasonally Adjusted)
Source: Federal Reserve via FRED (Series: M2SL). Notice the dramatic 2020-2021 surge — M2 grew over 25% year-over-year, driven by the combination of pandemic fiscal stimulus and Fed quantitative easing. M2 then declined briefly in 2022-2023 (the first sustained decline since the 1940s) as the Fed reduced its balance sheet, before resuming modest growth. The pandemic episode is the largest peacetime M2 expansion in American history.
Federal Reserve Balance Sheet (Total Assets, Billions)
Source: Federal Reserve via FRED (Series: WALCL). The Fed's balance sheet has gone through three structural eras: pre-2008 (around $900B, primarily Treasury securities); 2008-2014 quantitative easing (rising to $4.5T by 2014); 2020-2022 pandemic QE (peaking near $9T). Quantitative tightening since 2022 has reduced it to approximately $6.7T as of 2025. Each $1 increase in the balance sheet creates roughly $1 of new monetary base.

A Open-market operations and interest on reserves

Part B — Multiple Choice (Intermediate)
5. Brazil's Real Plan in 1994 succeeded primarily because:
A The government printed dramatically less money
B The currency was directly backed by gold
C A non-monetary unit of account (URV) was used to break inflation inertia before currency replacement
D Foreign aid covered the fiscal deficit
6. The Fed's balance sheet expanded from about $900B to $4.5T between 2008 and 2014 (quantitative easing). Inflation during this period was below target. The most plausible explanation is:
A The new reserves stayed parked at the Fed and were not loaned out into spending
B The Fed's actions were illegal and had no real effect
C Inflation was lagging and showed up later in 2022
D The CPI was systematically misreported during this period
7. Central bank independence is associated with:
A Higher average inflation but faster growth
B Lower average inflation with no apparent cost in growth
C Higher unemployment in all cases
D No measurable economic effects
Part C — Numerical Problems
8. An economy has $200 billion of currency in circulation, $1,800 billion in checking-account deposits, and $4,000 billion in savings deposits. (a) Compute M1. (b) Compute M2. (c) The reserve ratio across the banking system is 6%, and there is no excess reserve holding or cash leakage. What is the simple money multiplier? (d) What would the monetary base have to be to support this M1?
Model answer: (a) M1 = currency + demand deposits = $200B + $1,800B = $2,000B. (b) M2 = M1 + savings = $2,000B + $4,000B = $6,000B. (c) Money multiplier m = 1/R = 1/0.06 ≈ 16.67. (d) If M1 = $2,000B and m = 16.67, the monetary base must be M1/m = $2,000B/16.67 ≈ $120B. (In reality, real-world multipliers are lower because banks hold excess reserves and people hold cash.)
9. A central bank conducts an open-market operation: it purchases $50 billion of Treasury bonds from a private dealer. The dealer deposits the proceeds at a bank with a 5% reserve ratio. (a) By how much does the monetary base immediately rise? (b) Trace the first three rounds of deposit creation. (c) What is the total long-run M1 expansion if the simple money multiplier holds?
Model answer: (a) Monetary base rises immediately by $50B (the amount the Fed paid for the bonds). (b) Round 1: $50B deposited; bank keeps $2.5B (5%) and lends $47.5B. Round 2: $47.5B redeposited; bank keeps $2.375B and lends $45.125B. Round 3: $45.125B redeposited; bank keeps $2.256B and lends $42.869B. Running M1 expansion: $50 + $47.5 + $45.125 = $142.625B after three rounds. (c) Total: $50B × (1/0.05) = $1,000B = $1 trillion of new M1 in the long run. The original $50B of monetary base supports 20× as much M1 because R = 5%.
Part D — Policy Scenario
Scenario: A small developing country has had average inflation of 80% per year for the past five years. Multiple stabilization plans have failed. The new Finance Minister calls you for advice. Three options on the table: (1) a sharp 25% devaluation of the currency followed by a peg to the U.S. dollar; (2) full dollarization (eliminate the local currency, adopt the dollar as legal tender, as Ecuador did in 2000 and El Salvador in 2001); (3) a Brazil-style URV plan, introducing a stable parallel unit of account before currency replacement.
10. Write a 250-word memo evaluating the three options. Reference: the three functions of money, inflation inertia, the role of central bank credibility, and the costs of each path. Take a clear position.
Model answer (sketch): All three options aim at the same goal — restoring the unit-of-account and store-of-value functions of money — but they differ in cost and reversibility. Option 1 (devaluation + peg) has the worst track record. Pegs to the dollar without underlying fiscal discipline tend to break under speculative pressure (Argentina 2001, Russia 1998, Mexico 1994), often with devastating consequences. The country gives up monetary policy independence without gaining permanent credibility. Option 2 (dollarization) permanently solves the credibility problem but at a high cost: the country loses all monetary sovereignty, including the ability to act as lender of last resort to its own banks, and surrenders seigniorage revenue. Ecuador and El Salvador both saw inflation fall sharply but also lost the ability to respond to shocks. Option 3 (URV-style plan) preserves monetary sovereignty if successful, but requires extraordinary execution. Brazil's plan worked because the architects diagnosed inflation inertia correctly, secured political support, and committed to fiscal discipline simultaneously. Most countries would fail at this. Position: Recommend the URV-style plan only if (a) the government can credibly commit to fiscal discipline, and (b) high-quality technical talent is available to design and execute the transition. If either condition is doubtful, full dollarization is the safer choice — losing monetary sovereignty is a real cost, but living with chronic 80% inflation is a much larger one. The half-measure (devaluation + peg) is the worst of all worlds and should be rejected.

Chapter 12 · Part V: Money and Prices in the Long Run

Money Growth and Inflation

Why printing money causes prices to rise — usually, eventually, with long and variable lags. The theory that explains every hyperinflation in history, and one that almost called the 2022 U.S. inflation correctly

In July 1946, Hungarians experienced the worst inflation ever recorded in human history. Prices in Budapest were doubling roughly every 15 hours. The monthly inflation rate that month was approximately 4.19 × 1016 percent — that's about 41.9 quadrillion percent. To stay ahead of the price changes, workers were paid two and three times per day; the Hungarian National Bank printed banknotes faster than they could be distributed. The largest banknote ever printed by any country in history was issued during this episode: the 100 quintillion (1020) pengő note. A Hungarian who had saved 1 pengő in June 1945 found that, by July 1946, that same pengő was worth roughly 1 / 30 septillion of its original purchasing power. Lifetime savings were destroyed in 13 months.

What caused this disaster? After World War II, Hungary's economy had been devastated — about 40% of its capital stock destroyed. The new coalition government inherited massive war debts, reparations to the Soviet Union, and a population in desperate need of relief. Rather than raising taxes or cutting spending, the government turned to the printing press. The central bank issued more pengős, then more, then more, in a hopeful attempt to fund obligations and stimulate recovery. The pengős paid for goods, the goods bought back the pengős at higher prices, more pengős were needed, and the spiral began. The episode ended only when the government finally introduced a new currency, the forint, on August 1, 1946 — at a conversion rate of 4 × 1029 pengő to 1 forint — and committed credibly to stop printing.

Eighty years later, in 2020-2022, the United States ran a far milder version of the same experiment. The Federal Reserve, responding to the COVID-19 pandemic, expanded the M2 money supply by 26.8% year-over-year by February 2021 — the fastest peacetime money growth in U.S. history since the Fed was founded in 1913. About 18 months later, in June 2022, U.S. CPI inflation peaked at 9.1%, the highest in four decades. The connection between the two episodes is the topic of this chapter: the relationship between money growth and inflation. It is one of the oldest, most-tested, and most reliably predictive theories in macroeconomics. It is also one of the most often misunderstood.

✦ Sharma's Take

Milton Friedman's most famous claim — "inflation is always and everywhere a monetary phenomenon" — is one of the most-cited and most-abused propositions in economics. Cited because the long-run cross-country and historical record overwhelmingly supports it: every hyperinflation in history has been accompanied by rapid money creation, and no country has ever had high inflation while keeping its money supply growth disciplined. Abused because the relationship is not mechanical, not instant, and not visible in every short-run episode.

The 2008-2014 quantitative easing expanded the Fed's balance sheet from $900B to $4.5T and produced average inflation below the 2% target. The 2020-2021 expansion of M2 by 26.8% YoY, two years later, produced 9.1% inflation. The difference was not the size of the central bank's balance sheet — it was whether the new money flowed into actual spending. This chapter teaches you why the simple version of the theory works in the long run, why it sometimes seems to fail in the short run, and how to read monetary data the way an actual macroeconomist does.

— A.S.

12.1 The Classical Dichotomy: Real vs. Nominal Variables

📘 Principles Level — Two kinds of variables, two different stories

To understand the relationship between money and prices, we first need to draw a fundamental distinction that 18th-century economists called the classical dichotomy: economic variables come in two kinds, real and nominal, and money affects them differently in the long run.

📚 Real vs. Nominal — the most consequential distinction in macroeconomics
Nominal variables
Variables measured in current dollar (or other currency) units. Examples: nominal GDP, nominal wages, nominal interest rates, the price of a hamburger in dollars. These all change when the price level changes.
Real variables
Variables measured in physical units or in inflation-adjusted "constant" dollars. Examples: real GDP (output of actual goods and services), real wages (purchasing power of labor income), real interest rates, the relative price of a hamburger in terms of haircuts. These are not directly affected by changes in the price level alone.
Classical dichotomy
The principle that, in the long run, real variables are determined by real factors (technology, preferences, capital, labor, institutions — the topics of Part IV) and nominal variables are determined by the money supply. The two stories run on independent tracks in the long run.
Monetary neutrality
A direct consequence of the classical dichotomy. In the long run, changes in the money supply have no effect on real variables. Doubling the money supply doubles all prices and nominal wages, leaving relative prices, real output, and real wages unchanged. Often summarized as: "money is a veil over the real economy."
⚠ Common Pitfall — "Monetary neutrality means money doesn't matter"

A frequent student misreading. Monetary neutrality applies to the long run only. In the short run, money very much affects real variables: monetary policy can fight recessions, lower unemployment, and accelerate growth (chapters 19-22). But these short-run real effects fade. Over a horizon of, say, ten years, an economy with 10% money growth will have roughly 10% higher prices than an economy with 0% money growth, but the same real output (assuming everything else is held constant).

Why does the long run differ from the short run? In the short run, prices and wages are sticky — they don't adjust instantly to monetary changes. So monetary expansion temporarily raises real spending, employment, and output. But over time, prices and wages adjust upward to match the larger money supply, and the real-economy effects unwind. By the time everyone has adjusted, only the price level has permanently changed. This is why this chapter is in the "long run" part of the textbook: we're tracing the destination, not the path.

12.2 The Quantity Theory of Money

📘 Principles Level — A 400-year-old theory that still works

The relationship between money and prices was first articulated clearly in the 16th century, when European economies experienced sustained inflation following the influx of silver and gold from the New World. The Spanish economist Martín de Azpilcueta noted in 1556 that the prices of goods rose roughly in proportion to the money supply. Over the following four centuries, the relationship was refined, formalized, and tested by John Locke, David Hume, Irving Fisher, and most influentially Milton Friedman. The mathematical statement of the theory has remained essentially unchanged since Fisher's The Purchasing Power of Money (1911).

The core idea is captured in the equation of exchange:

⚙ Equation Walkthrough — The Equation of Exchange
M × V = P × Y
M
Money supply — typically M2 in modern usage. The total stock of money in the economy at a point in time.
V
Velocity of money — the average number of times each dollar is spent on final goods and services per year. Computed as V = (P × Y) / M. Velocity reflects how rapidly money circulates: the higher V, the more transactions a given money stock can support.
P
Price level — typically the GDP deflator or CPI. The economy-wide average price of goods and services.
Y
Real GDP — total output of goods and services in real (inflation-adjusted) terms. The same Y from chapter 7.
P × Y
Nominal GDP — the total dollar value of all final goods and services produced. The right-hand side of the equation is total spending on output.

Reading it in plain language: the total stock of money (M) multiplied by how often each dollar gets spent (V) must equal the total dollar value of all transactions on final output (P × Y). This is an identity — it must hold by definition because V is constructed to make it hold. It becomes a theory only when we add an assumption about V.

The U.S. velocity, by way of context: if 2024 nominal GDP was about $29 trillion and M2 was about $21 trillion, then V ≈ 29/21 ≈ 1.4. Each dollar of M2 was spent about 1.4 times that year on final goods and services. Historically, U.S. M2 velocity ran around 1.7–2.1 from the 1960s through 2000, then fell sharply during and after the 2008 GFC, dropping further to about 1.13 in mid-2020 during the pandemic.

⚙ Equation Walkthrough — From Identity to Theory
%ΔM + %ΔV = %ΔP + %ΔY
%ΔM
Percentage growth rate of the money supply.
%ΔV
Percentage growth rate of velocity.
%ΔP
Percentage growth rate of the price level — i.e., the inflation rate (π).
%ΔY
Percentage growth rate of real output (real GDP growth).

How we go from identity to theory: the equation MV = PY mathematically implies that the growth rates also add up — taking logs and differentiating gives %ΔM + %ΔV = %ΔP + %ΔY. This is still an identity. It becomes the quantity theory of money when we make two specific assumptions:

  1. Velocity is approximately stable in the long run: %ΔV ≈ 0. People's habits about how often they spend their money cash holdings change slowly, mostly due to the development of credit cards, electronic payments, and similar institutional innovations.
  2. Real output growth is determined by real factors (technology, capital, labor — chapter 9), not by money. So in the long run, %ΔY is given exogenously.

With these assumptions, the equation simplifies to: π ≈ %ΔM − %ΔY. Inflation equals money growth minus real GDP growth. If money grows 8% per year and real output grows 2%, prices rise about 6% per year. This is the central prediction of the quantity theory.

📐 Worked Example 12.1 — Predicting inflation from money growth
An economy has nominal GDP of $20 trillion, M2 of $14 trillion, and real GDP growth of 2.5% per year. The central bank announces it will increase M2 by 9% over the next year. (a) Compute current velocity. (b) Assuming velocity stays constant, what will the inflation rate be? (c) What money growth rate would the central bank need to hit a 2% inflation target?
1
(a) Velocity: V = (P × Y) / M = $20T / $14T ≈ 1.43. Each dollar of M2 supports about $1.43 of nominal GDP per year.
2
(b) Inflation prediction: using the simplified quantity theory, π ≈ %ΔM − %ΔY = 9% − 2.5% = 6.5% — well above target.
3
(c) Required money growth for 2% inflation: %ΔM = π + %ΔY = 2% + 2.5% = 4.5%
To hit a 2% inflation target with 2.5% real growth, the central bank needs to grow M2 at about 4.5% per year — not zero, because some money growth is needed to support the rising real volume of transactions. This is roughly the rule of thumb the Federal Reserve uses internally, though the Fed's official inflation target is operationalized through interest rates and the PCE price index, not through M2 targets directly.
✏ Try This — A failure of the simple theory

Between 2008 and 2014, the Fed's monetary base (M0) rose roughly fivefold, from about $900 billion to $4.5 trillion. According to the simplest version of the quantity theory, this should have caused massive inflation. CPI inflation over that period averaged about 1.6% — well below target. What does this tell us about the assumptions underlying the quantity theory?

▸ Reveal answer
The 5× expansion was in the monetary base (M0), not in M2 or M1 — the new reserves stayed parked at the Fed and were not lent out into the broader economy. M2 over the same period rose only about 60% (about 8% per year), and velocity fell sharply, so total nominal spending did not surge. The lesson: the quantity theory requires (a) the right monetary aggregate (M2 or broader, not M0), and (b) reasonably stable velocity. Both assumptions failed in 2008-2014. The 2020-2022 episode was different in both respects: M2 grew at record rates AND the new money was actively spent (via fiscal stimulus), so velocity didn't drop enough to absorb it. Inflation followed within 18 months, exactly as a careful quantity theorist would have predicted.

12.3 Hungary 1946: The Worst Hyperinflation Ever Recorded

1945–1946 Hungary Hyperinflation record

Prices Doubling Every 15 Hours: The Pengő Crisis

By the end of World War II, Hungary's economy lay in ruins. Roughly 40% of the country's capital stock had been destroyed in the fighting between Nazi German forces and the advancing Soviet Red Army. The country had been on the losing side of the war and was required to pay $300 million in reparations and to cover the living costs of a 700,000-strong Soviet occupying force. The new coalition government inherited devastated industry, depleted foreign reserves, and an exhausted population that needed immediate relief.

Faced with overwhelming financial obligations and few good options, the postwar Hungarian government chose the path that has destroyed many currencies: it ordered the central bank to print money. Initially, the goal was modest — fund reconstruction, pay wages, cover the reparations. But once it became clear that the printing press was the path of least political resistance, money creation accelerated. The monthly inflation rate rose from a high but manageable 50% in late 1945 to 207% per day at the peak in July 1946. At that rate, prices doubled approximately every 15 hours. Workers were paid two and three times daily because the morning's wages had lost half their value by evening.

The denominations of pengő banknotes tell the story most vividly. Before 1945, the largest banknote in Hungary was 1,000 pengő. By the end of 1945, it was 10 million pengő. By the middle of 1946, the central bank issued the 100 quintillion (1020) pengő note — the largest-denomination banknote ever printed by any government in history. A separate "tax pengő" was introduced as a parallel index for tax payments. People stopped using the currency for any transaction that could be conducted in barter; cigarettes and gold became informal media of exchange.

The crisis ended only when the government finally committed to a credible stabilization. On August 1, 1946, a new currency — the forint — was introduced at a conversion rate of 4 × 1029 (400 octillion) pengő to 1 forint. Crucially, the new currency was paired with strict commitments to stop printing money for budget financing and to back the forint with tangible reserves. Industry had partially recovered by then, the Soviet occupation forces had stabilized, and credibility could be restored. Within a few months, prices in forint terms were stable. They remained reasonably stable for the next two decades. The pengő itself ceased to exist on July 31, 1946, as one of the most worthless currencies ever printed.

What this case illustrates with force: the quantity theory's central prediction works at extreme amplitudes. When money supply growth rates run into the millions of percent per month, every other economic factor becomes irrelevant. Velocity, real output, supply chains — all are dwarfed by the avalanche of new currency. The hyperinflation episode is not a disproof of the quantity theory; it is the most extreme confirmation imaginable. What no theory can address is the political dynamic that allows a government to keep printing money long enough to reach this point.

12.4 The U.S. 2020–2022 Episode: A Modern Test of the Theory

2020–2022 United States Modern monetary test

Did Friedman's Theory Hold? The "Transitory" Debate Settled

When COVID-19 struck the United States in March 2020, the policy response was on a scale not seen since World War II. The Federal Reserve cut rates to zero, launched a massive asset purchase program, and expanded its balance sheet from $4.2 trillion to over $9 trillion within two years. Simultaneously, Congress passed three major fiscal stimulus packages totaling about $5 trillion — direct cash payments to households, expanded unemployment insurance, and small-business support. Between the two, the U.S. M2 money supply grew at 26.8% year-over-year by February 2021, the fastest peacetime money growth in U.S. history since the Fed was established in 1913.

What happened next is now a textbook case in the validity (and limits) of the quantity theory. Throughout 2021, U.S. inflation began to rise — first in goods prices affected by supply-chain disruptions, then more broadly. Most policymakers and forecasters, including the Federal Reserve, characterized the inflation as "transitory": a temporary product of supply-chain bottlenecks and shifted demand patterns that would resolve as the economy normalized. By mid-2021, with CPI inflation already at 5.4%, the Fed continued to expect inflation to fall back below 3% within a year.

A small group of economists, working from a Friedmanite quantity-theory framework, predicted otherwise. They argued that 26% M2 growth, combined with massive fiscal stimulus that put new money into spending hands rather than into bank vaults, would inevitably raise prices — but with the "long and variable lag" Friedman had described, typically 18-24 months. Their prediction: CPI inflation would peak around 9% in mid-2022. CPI inflation peaked at 9.1% in June 2022, almost exactly 18 months after the M2 growth peak. The "transitory" framing was abandoned; Fed Chair Jerome Powell formally retired the word in November 2021.

Two important caveats prevent us from declaring complete victory for the quantity theory:

  • Other factors mattered. Russia's invasion of Ukraine in February 2022 spiked oil and food prices through commodity-market channels independent of monetary growth. Supply-chain disruptions raised goods prices. These contributed perhaps 2-3 percentage points to peak inflation.
  • Velocity was not stable. M2 velocity had collapsed during the pandemic from 1.4 to 1.1, then partially rebounded as spending normalized. The simple quantity-theory formula assumes constant velocity, which clearly didn't hold.

Even with these caveats, the modern Hungarian-style test was a clean win for monetarist analysis. After the 2008 QE, money supply growth (M2) was modest, velocity collapsed, and inflation stayed low — consistent with the theory. After the 2020 stimulus, money supply growth was extreme, velocity didn't fully absorb it, and inflation surged with an 18-month lag — also consistent with the theory. "Inflation is always and everywhere a monetary phenomenon" still appears to hold, provided we use the right monetary aggregate, allow for long lags, and account for non-monetary shocks.

✦ Sharma's Take — Why "transitory" was the wrong mental model

The Fed's "transitory" framing in 2021 was not, strictly speaking, wrong about the supply-side disruptions. Bottlenecks really were resolving; shifted demand really was normalizing. The error was treating the 2020-2021 monetary expansion as if it had no consequences for medium-term inflation. The Fed had become accustomed, over the 2008-2019 period, to a world in which large monetary expansions did not produce much inflation — because banks held excess reserves and velocity was falling. They projected this experience onto the 2020-2021 episode without recognizing that the difference was crucial: this time, the new money was put into spending hands via direct fiscal payments, not into bank reserves via Treasury purchases.

The lesson: institutional memory and recent experience are dangerous guides during structural breaks. Macroeconomics requires constantly checking whether the assumptions behind your favorite theory are holding. The quantity theory framework is one of the field's oldest and most durable; abandoning it because it didn't fit 2008-2014 was a mistake the Fed admitted in retrospect.

— A.S.

12.5 Inflation as a Tax: The Quiet Wealth Transfer

📘 Principles Level — Why governments love inflation in private

When a government creates money to fund spending, the resulting inflation transfers real resources from holders of money and government bonds to the government itself. This transfer is called the inflation tax (or "seigniorage" when applied to currency creation). It is one of the oldest forms of public finance — used by Roman emperors, medieval monarchs, and modern democratic governments alike.

The mechanism is simple. Suppose you hold $10,000 in cash or in a non-interest-bearing checking account. If inflation runs at 5% per year, the real value of your money falls by $500 per year — 5% of $10,000. That $500 of lost purchasing power doesn't disappear; it accrues to whoever issued the new money that drove the inflation. In modern economies, that means the government, which receives the resources by issuing new currency or by having the central bank buy government bonds with newly created reserves.

The inflation tax has three properties that make it politically attractive:

  1. It requires no legislation. No congressional vote, no public debate, no electoral accountability.
  2. It is invisible. Most people don't realize they're paying it — they blame "the cost of living going up" rather than recognizing the government is the beneficiary.
  3. It is regressive. Wealthy households hold most of their wealth in inflation-protected assets (real estate, stocks, indexed bonds). Poor households disproportionately hold cash and have wages that don't immediately adjust. The inflation tax falls hardest on the financially least sophisticated.
⚙ Equation Walkthrough — The Inflation Tax Revenue
Inflation tax revenue = π × (M / P)
π
The inflation rate. The annual rate at which the price level rises.
M / P
Real money balances — the inflation-adjusted value of the money supply. Equal to the total purchasing power that the public is currently holding in monetary form.
π × (M/P)
The total real wealth that holders of money lose each year to inflation. This is exactly the real resources the government receives from creating new money.

Intuition: if the public holds $20 trillion in real money balances and inflation is 5%, then $1 trillion of real purchasing power is transferred from the public to the money issuer each year. That's the inflation tax.

The Laffer-curve aspect: raising the inflation rate doesn't proportionally raise inflation tax revenue, because at higher inflation rates people hold less real money (they switch to alternative stores of value, foreign currency, or barter). At very high inflation, real money balances collapse — the tax base shrinks faster than the rate rises. This is one reason hyperinflations are economically self-defeating even for the government that started them.

📐 Worked Example 12.2 — Computing the inflation tax
A country has M2 of $4 trillion (in real terms). The government plans to fund a $200 billion deficit next year through new money creation. (a) What inflation rate would this require, assuming people continue to hold the same real money balances? (b) Why is this assumption optimistic — what would actually happen?
1
(a) Required inflation: we need π × $4T = $200B, so π = $200B / $4,000B = 5%. The government would need 5% inflation to extract $200B in real resources from money holders.
2
(b) Why the assumption fails: if 5% inflation persists, people respond by holding less real money — shifting to inflation-protected assets, foreign currency, or simply spending faster. Real money balances M/P fall. To maintain $200B in real revenue, the government needs to inflate faster than 5%. This raises real money balances even less, requiring still faster inflation. The dynamic feedback is exactly what generates hyperinflation: governments keep raising the inflation tax rate, but the tax base keeps collapsing, until the currency loses its function entirely. Hungary 1946 is the limit case.
The inflation tax is bounded by the public's willingness to hold money. A government targeting modest amounts of inflation tax revenue (say, 1-2% of GDP at low inflation rates) can do so quietly and persistently — this is roughly what happens in countries with chronic 5-10% inflation. A government trying to extract larger amounts triggers the feedback loop and slides into crisis. This is the central theoretical insight that explains why hyperinflations always start with fiscal desperation and only end with credible commitments to fiscal discipline.

12.6 The Fisher Effect: Inflation and Nominal Interest Rates

📘 Principles Level — Why bond yields jump when inflation rises

From chapter 8 you already know that the real interest rate equals the nominal rate minus the inflation rate: r = i − π. The Fisher effect (named after Irving Fisher, who articulated it in 1930) takes this one step further: in the long run, nominal interest rates adjust one-for-one with inflation expectations.

The intuition is straightforward. Lenders care about real returns — what their money will actually buy when repaid. If lenders expect 5% inflation, they will demand a nominal rate that gives them their target real return plus 5% to compensate for the lost purchasing power. Borrowers, expecting their nominal income to rise with inflation, are willing to pay the higher nominal rate. So when expected inflation rises by 1 percentage point, nominal rates rise by 1 percentage point.

⚙ Equation Walkthrough — The Fisher Effect
i = r + πe (in the long run, with stable real rates)
i
Nominal interest rate — what the bond actually pays.
r
Real interest rate — determined in the long run by real factors (capital productivity, time preference, savings supply) per chapter 10's loanable funds model. In the U.S. historically, this has averaged about 1-2%.
πe
Expected inflation rate. Critically, what matters for the nominal rate is what people expect inflation to be — not necessarily what it turns out to be.

Reading the relationship: if the real rate is 2% and expected inflation is 3%, the nominal rate should be about 5%. If expected inflation rises to 8%, the nominal rate should rise to 10% — the same real rate plus higher inflation compensation.

The Volcker disinflation as a Fisher effect demonstration: in the late 1970s, U.S. inflation expectations were running at 10-13%, and 10-year Treasury yields rose above 15%. As Paul Volcker's Fed crushed inflation between 1980 and 1983, expectations gradually came down, and 10-year yields fell — though with a lag, since lenders required time to be convinced that low inflation would persist. By the late 1990s, with expectations anchored at 2-3%, 10-year yields had fallen to 5-6%. The relationship has been visible in U.S. data for 40+ years.

⚠ Common Pitfall — "The Fed sets interest rates, so the Fisher effect doesn't apply"

A common student confusion. The Federal Reserve sets the federal funds rate, a very short-term overnight rate. The Fisher effect concerns longer-term nominal interest rates determined by markets — 10-year Treasury yields, mortgage rates, corporate bond yields. The Fed has only indirect influence on long rates, primarily through its effect on inflation expectations.

If markets believe the Fed will keep inflation near 2%, long-term rates will stay relatively low even when the Fed raises short-term rates. If markets lose faith and expect 8% inflation, long-term rates will jump above where the Fed wants them — and the Fed will be unable to lower them without creating yet more inflation. This is why central bank credibility is so valuable: a credible central bank can set short rates without losing control of the long end of the yield curve. A non-credible one cannot.

12.7 The Costs of Inflation

📘 Principles Level — Why moderate inflation matters less than people think, and a lot more than economists once thought

If inflation simply raises all prices and wages proportionally, why do people complain so much about it? In a perfectly indexed economy with rational expectations, inflation should impose only minor costs. But real economies are far from perfectly indexed, and the costs are larger and more varied than a simple model suggests.

The seven main costs of inflation, ordered from smallest to largest
CostWhat it isHow big it is
Shoe-leather costs The hassle of trying to hold less cash and make more frequent trips to the bank — the literal "wearing out of shoe leather" from extra trips. Trivial in modern economies with electronic banking. Significant only in hyperinflation.
Menu costs The cost of changing prices in catalogs, menus, websites, contracts. Higher inflation means more frequent reprinting, renegotiation, recoding. Modest in moderate inflation. Becomes severe at high inflation when prices change weekly or daily.
Misallocation from relative-price variability Different prices adjust at different speeds, so high inflation distorts relative prices. Producers and consumers make worse decisions because the price signals are noisy. Important and underappreciated. Inflation above ~5% measurably impairs economic efficiency.
Tax distortions The U.S. tax code is not fully indexed for inflation. Capital gains, interest income, and depreciation allowances are based on nominal values. Higher inflation → effectively higher real tax rates on capital. Significant. Estimated at roughly 0.1-0.2 percentage points of real GDP per year per percentage point of inflation above target.
Confusion and cost of accounting Businesses, households, and governments must constantly distinguish nominal from real changes. Decisions become harder; mistakes more frequent. Moderate but persistent.
Arbitrary redistribution Unexpected inflation transfers wealth from creditors to debtors, from workers with sticky wages to employers, from those on fixed nominal incomes (retirees) to those whose income adjusts. Large in episodes of unexpected inflation. The 1970s inflation in the U.S. transferred trillions in real wealth from bondholders to borrowers.
Loss of price stability and central bank credibility Once inflation expectations become unanchored, regaining them requires deep recessions (per chapter 22). The Volcker disinflation cost the U.S. economy several years of high unemployment. By far the largest cost. The disinflation of 1980-1983 cost roughly $1 trillion in lost output (in 2024 dollars).
✦ Sharma's Take — Why central banks fight even small inflation overshoots

The standard textbook treatment of inflation costs makes them look modest. Shoe leather, menu costs — these are minor inconveniences. So why does the Fed fight tooth and nail to keep inflation at 2% rather than letting it run at 4%? The answer lies in the bottom row of the table: credibility, once lost, is enormously expensive to regain.

Once inflation expectations come unmoored — once households and firms start writing higher inflation into their wage demands and price setting — pulling them back requires the central bank to demonstrate it will tolerate substantial economic pain to restore the anchor. The 1980-1983 Volcker disinflation pushed unemployment to 10.8% and caused the worst recession since the Great Depression to that point. The benefit was decades of low inflation. But the cost was real: roughly $1 trillion in lost output. Compared to that downside, fighting a brewing inflation early at the cost of a quarter-point overshoot is a bargain.

This is why the 2021-2022 Fed delay in raising rates was so heavily criticized in retrospect. Once inflation broke 5% and started showing up in wage demands, the disinflation effort became dramatically more expensive than it would have been if the Fed had moved 6-12 months earlier. Modern monetary policy is partly about technical management, but mostly about preserving an institutional asset: credibility.

— A.S.

12.8 Interactive: The Quantity Theory in Action

🎛 Interactive — Money Growth, Velocity, and Inflation

Drag the sliders to see how monetary changes propagate to prices

What you're seeing: the chart shows the four growth rates that must satisfy the quantity equation %ΔM + %ΔV = %ΔP + %ΔY. The implied inflation rate (%ΔP) is computed automatically and color-coded: green for stable (0-3%), gold for elevated (3-7%), red for high (>7%), and a special "hyperinflation" warning for very high rates.

Try: set money growth to 27% and velocity change to −5% (this approximates 2021 conditions). Inflation should come out around 19%, but if you account for the supply-chain absorption of about 10 percentage points, you land near the actual 9% U.S. peak. Then try money growth 0% and velocity change −15% — that's roughly 2008-2014, where inflation stayed below target despite huge monetary base expansion.

12.9 The Cross-Country Evidence

📘 Principles Level — What 50 years of data look like

One way to test the quantity theory is to compare countries. If the theory is correct, countries with persistently higher money growth should have persistently higher inflation. The data overwhelmingly support this prediction at long time horizons.

Robert Barro and others have documented the relationship in dozens of empirical studies. Among countries averaging less than 10% annual money growth (most developed economies), the M-π relationship is noisy because velocity changes and other factors dominate. Among countries with sustained money growth above 20%, the relationship becomes nearly mechanical. Argentina, Turkey, Venezuela, Zimbabwe, Iran — all have experienced both rapid money growth and high inflation, in close numerical correspondence over multi-year averages.

The clearest demonstration: no country in modern history has experienced sustained high inflation without rapid money growth, and no country with disciplined money growth has experienced sustained high inflation. There are short-run exceptions (a year or two), but no medium-run ones. Friedman's claim survives empirical scrutiny, even as the short-run mechanics get messier than the simple theory suggests.

🎮 MacroNations Connection — Inflation Targeting

In MacroNations, the Stability pillar (200 points) rewards keeping inflation close to the 2% target. Your Money Supply Growth lever directly determines long-run inflation per the quantity equation in the simulation engine: an M2 growth rate of 4-5% per year typically produces 2% inflation, given the simulation's built-in 2-3% real growth trend. Push money growth above 7-8%, and inflation begins to rise; above 15%, you risk losing inflation expectations and triggering the Volcker-style stabilization scenario where pulling them back costs you both Stability and GDP points for several turns.

The simulation also models the Fisher effect: your nominal interest rate lever influences real rates only when inflation expectations are anchored. Once expectations destabilize, raising the nominal rate further only raises the inflation tax base, accelerating the dynamics rather than damping them. This is why the simulation rewards disciplined monetary growth as a long-run strategy rather than reactive tightening as a short-run fix.

U.S. M2 Money Supply, Year-over-Year Growth Rate (%)
Source: Federal Reserve via FRED (Series: M2SL, year-over-year change). Notice the extraordinary 2020-2021 episode — M2 YoY growth peaked at 26.8% in February 2021, the fastest peacetime rate in U.S. history. Then in 2022-2023 M2 actually contracted year-over-year for the first time since the early 1990s, falling about 4% YoY at its trough. This is the most dramatic monetary cycle in seventy years.
U.S. CPI Inflation, Year-over-Year (%)
Source: BLS via FRED (Series: CPIAUCSL, year-over-year change). Note the 18-month lag from M2 growth in the chart above: M2 growth peaked February 2021, CPI inflation peaked June 2022 at 9.1%. Compare to the 2008-2014 era, when M2 growth was modest and CPI inflation stayed below the 2% target despite massive expansion of the monetary base — the difference between "money in bank reserves" and "money in spending hands."

Chapter 12 Quiz

Quantity theory, inflation tax, Fisher effect, costs of inflation
Part A — Multiple Choice (Principles)
1. The classical dichotomy holds that, in the long run:
A Real and nominal variables both move with the money supply
B Real variables are determined by real factors; nominal variables by the money supply
C The money supply has no effect on any variables
D Real variables are always determined by central bank policy
2. The quantity equation MV = PY says that:
A Money supply equals nominal GDP
B Money supply times velocity equals nominal GDP
C Inflation always equals money growth
D The price level is constant in the long run
3. Hungary's 1946 hyperinflation peaked at prices doubling approximately every:
A 15 minutes
B 15 hours
C 15 days
D 15 months
4. The Fisher effect states that, in the long run, a 1 percentage point rise in expected inflation should:
A Raise nominal interest rates by 1 percentage point, leaving real rates unchanged
B Lower nominal interest rates by 1 percentage point
C Have no effect on interest rates
D Raise real interest rates by 1 percentage point
Part B — Multiple Choice (Intermediate)
5. The U.S. M2 money supply grew at a peak rate of about 26.8% YoY in February 2021. Per the simple quantity theory with stable velocity and 2.5% real GDP growth, this would predict inflation around:
A 2%
B 9%
C 24%
D 50%
6. The Fed's 2008-2014 quantitative easing dramatically expanded the monetary base but produced inflation below 2%. The most plausible reason is:
A The quantity theory was disproved during this episode
B The new reserves stayed parked at the Fed and didn't expand the broader money supply (M2) or active spending
C Inflation was secretly hidden by the BLS in this period
D Real GDP growth was high enough to absorb all the new money
7. The single largest economic cost of high inflation, as discussed in this chapter, is:
A Shoe-leather costs
B Menu costs
C The cost of restoring central bank credibility once inflation expectations become unanchored
D Confusion in accounting
Part C — Numerical Problems
8. An economy has nominal GDP of $25 trillion, M2 of $20 trillion, and real GDP growth of 2% per year. (a) Compute current velocity. (b) Assuming velocity is constant, what money growth rate is needed to hit a 2% inflation target? (c) The central bank actually expands M2 by 12% next year. What inflation rate does the simple quantity theory predict?
Model answer: (a) V = ($25T) / ($20T) = 1.25 — each dollar of M2 supports $1.25 of nominal GDP per year. (b) For 2% inflation with 2% real growth, money growth must be π + %ΔY = 2% + 2% = 4%. (c) With 12% money growth: π = %ΔM − %ΔY = 12% − 2% = 10% inflation — well above target.
9. A government holds nominal Treasury debt of $30 trillion, with an average maturity of 5 years and average coupon rate of 3%. Inflation unexpectedly rises from 2% (anticipated) to 7% (realized) and stays at 7% for 5 years. (a) What was the expected real interest rate on the debt? (b) What is the realized real rate? (c) What real wealth transfer occurs from creditors to the government over 5 years? (Approximate, ignoring discounting.)
Model answer: (a) Expected real rate = i − π_e = 3% − 2% = 1%. (b) Realized real rate = i − π_realized = 3% − 7% = −4%. (c) Each year, creditors lose 5 percentage points more than expected on $30T = $1.5 trillion in real wealth. Over 5 years (ignoring rollover effects and discounting): roughly $7.5 trillion in real wealth transferred from bondholders to the government. This is exactly the dynamic of the 1970s-1980s U.S. inflation: bondholders who had bought long-duration debt at 5-7% nominal yields under expectations of 2-3% inflation got blindsided by 10%+ realized inflation. Trillions in real wealth quietly moved from creditors (including foreign governments holding U.S. Treasuries) to the U.S. government and to leveraged borrowers like homeowners with fixed-rate mortgages.
Part D — Policy Scenario
Scenario: A small developing country has run persistent fiscal deficits financed by central bank money creation. Annual M2 growth has averaged 30% for five years; CPI inflation now runs at 22% per year; the currency is weakening rapidly. The central bank is technically independent but in practice answers to the Finance Minister. The IMF is offering a stabilization package conditional on (1) immediate fiscal balance (within 18 months), (2) granting the central bank statutory independence, and (3) a one-time 30% currency devaluation. Hardline ministers reject the conditions as politically impossible. Reformers argue the country must accept them or risk hyperinflation.
10. Write a 250-word policy memo for the President. Reference: the quantity theory, the inflation tax dynamics, central bank independence (chapter 11), the long-run cost of restoring credibility, and the 1946 Hungarian or 1994 Brazilian precedents. Take a clear position.
Model answer (sketch): The country is on the precipice of hyperinflation but has not yet crossed it. With M2 growth at 30% and inflation at 22%, real money balances are being eroded faster than the inflation tax base can replenish them; the dynamic is heading in only one direction. Two outcomes are possible: (1) accept stabilization now at moderate cost; (2) wait, slide into hyperinflation, and pay enormously larger costs later. The IMF package, while politically painful, is the right path because: (i) the quantity theory predicts that 30% money growth will generate 25-30% inflation indefinitely — fiscal balance is necessary to stop money creation; (ii) Brazil's 1994 Real Plan and Hungary's 1946 forint stabilization both required fiscal discipline plus credible monetary commitment — neither alone is sufficient; (iii) statutory central bank independence anchors expectations, providing a credibility floor without which any plan will fail; (iv) the one-time 30% devaluation absorbs accumulated overvaluation in one shot rather than letting markets do it via continued depreciation. The cost of waiting is far higher. Every six months delayed adds, conservatively, 2-3 years of disinflationary recession to the eventual stabilization. The hardliners' "politically impossible" framing reflects political-economy preferences, not economic reality. Position: accept the IMF package immediately, supplement it with a Real Plan-style transitional unit-of-account if needed, and invest political capital in selling the package to the public as an alternative to far worse alternatives. Hyperinflation is a far larger political risk than IMF conditionality.
