Chapter 13 · Part VI: The Macroeconomics of Open Economies

Open-Economy Macroeconomics

Why the exchange rate is the most consequential price in any economy that trades, why countries can't have everything they want in international finance, and how a single 4:30 AM announcement in Zurich erased $400 billion of value in 90 seconds

At 4:30 AM Eastern Time on Thursday, January 15, 2015, the Swiss National Bank issued a press release. The release was three sentences long. It announced that the SNB was abandoning the floor it had maintained for three and a half years that kept the Swiss franc from rising above 1.20 per euro. Within ninety seconds, the franc was up 30% against the euro — the largest single-day move ever recorded for a major developed-country currency. By the end of the day, several large forex brokers were insolvent. Hedge funds had lost billions. Swiss watch maker Swatch's CEO called the move "a tsunami." Approximately $400 billion in market value had been redistributed in an event that lasted less than two minutes from announcement to peak.

What had the SNB been doing for the previous three and a half years? They had been printing Swiss francs to buy euros — millions per minute, billions per day — to prevent the franc from appreciating beyond their 1.20 floor. Why? Because Switzerland is a small open economy surrounded by the larger Eurozone, and during the European debt crisis (2010-2012), nervous capital had flooded into Switzerland as a safe haven, threatening to crush Swiss exporters by making their products too expensive abroad. By January 2015, the SNB owned foreign currency reserves equal to roughly 70% of Swiss GDP — an unprecedented balance sheet that grew every day it kept defending the floor. The decision to stop defending it was driven less by a sudden change in heart than by the looming European Central Bank announcement of a massive new quantitative easing program — which would have made defending the peg dramatically more expensive.

This single event illustrates virtually every major concept in this chapter: capital flows driving currency movements; the impossible trinity that forces countries to choose two of three desirable policies; the balance sheet costs of currency intervention; the spillover effects of one country's monetary policy on another's; and the real-economy consequences of exchange rate changes (Swiss exports, hedge fund losses, real wages, tourism). By the end of this chapter you'll be able to read a news story about a currency crisis and understand exactly what is being traded, who is winning, who is losing, and what policy options the country has left.

✦ Sharma's Take

For decades, macroeconomics textbooks treated "open economy" topics as an afterthought — a chapter or two at the end, after the "real" material on growth, money, and business cycles. That treatment is wrong. Every country in the modern world is open in some meaningful sense. Capital flows globally. Goods cross borders. Currencies trade 24 hours a day with daily volumes exceeding $7.5 trillion — that's about $7.5 trillion per day, vastly larger than the underlying flow of goods trade and several times annual world GDP.

This chapter is short because the conceptual machinery is compact — half a dozen key concepts and one impossible-to-violate constraint (the trilemma). But the conceptual machinery is enormously powerful. Once you internalize how trade flows, capital flows, and exchange rates fit together, you can analyze nearly any open-economy policy question — from EU adoption to dollarization to currency wars to capital controls. The Switzerland 2015 case lets us walk through almost every concept with one vivid example.

— A.S.

13.1 The Three Big Concepts: Trade, Capital, and Exchange Rates

📘 Principles Level — The three flows that define an open economy

An open economy interacts with the rest of the world along three dimensions. Goods and services cross borders (exports and imports). Capital crosses borders (foreigners buying domestic assets; residents buying foreign assets). And the exchange rate — the price at which one currency converts into another — adjusts to make all of this possible.

These three flows are tightly linked. When American consumers buy more Japanese cars, they ultimately pay in yen, which means somebody is selling dollars for yen, which puts downward pressure on the dollar relative to the yen. When foreign investors decide American Treasury bonds are safer than European ones, they sell euros for dollars, pushing the dollar up. The exchange rate is the price that makes all of these markets clear simultaneously.

📚 Key Terms — defined here, used throughout the chapter
Exports (X)
Goods and services produced domestically and sold to foreigners. A Boeing 737 sold to an Indian airline is a U.S. export. Includes both physical goods and services (consulting, software, tourism revenue from foreign visitors).
Imports (M)
Goods and services produced abroad and bought by domestic residents. A Toyota Camry assembled in Japan and sold in the U.S. is a U.S. import.
Net exports (NX)
NX = X − M. The trade balance. Positive when a country exports more than it imports (trade surplus); negative when it imports more (trade deficit). The same NX from chapter 7's Y = C + I + G + NX.
Capital outflow (CO) / Net capital outflow (NCO)
The net amount of domestic residents buying foreign assets minus foreigners buying domestic assets. Positive when residents are net lenders to the world; negative when foreigners are net lenders to us. A country running a trade deficit must, by accounting identity, be a net capital importer.
Nominal exchange rate (e)
The rate at which one currency converts into another. If 1 USD = 1.10 CHF, then e = 1.10 CHF/USD. There are two equivalent ways to quote this (CHF per USD, or USD per CHF), and you must always check which one is being used.
Real exchange rate (ε)
The nominal exchange rate adjusted for relative price levels: ε = e × P / P*, where P is the domestic price level and P* is the foreign price level. Measures the relative cost of goods across countries, not just currencies.
Appreciation / Depreciation
A currency appreciates when it can buy more foreign currency (gets stronger). It depreciates when it can buy less (gets weaker). Important: if quoted as "foreign per domestic" (e.g., CHF per USD), an appreciation means the number gets bigger; if quoted as "domestic per foreign" (e.g., USD per CHF), an appreciation means the number gets smaller. Always check which way the rate is quoted.
Pegged / floating exchange rate
A pegged rate is fixed by the central bank at a target value (e.g., Saudi riyal = 3.75/USD since 1986). A floating rate is determined by supply and demand in foreign-exchange markets (e.g., USD/JPY). Many countries operate in between (managed float): they let the rate move but intervene at extremes.
⚠ Common Pitfall — "A strong currency is always good"

Politicians often boast about a "strong dollar" or "strong yuan" as if currency appreciation were automatically beneficial. The reality is more nuanced. A strong currency:

  • Helps consumers and importers — foreign goods become cheaper.
  • Helps domestic residents traveling or buying foreign assets — their currency stretches further.
  • Hurts exporters — their products become more expensive abroad, reducing competitiveness.
  • Hurts domestic producers competing with imports — foreign rivals look relatively cheaper.
  • Reduces nominal GDP growth indirectly through lower NX.

Whether "strong" is good depends on which side of these trade-offs your economy is on. Switzerland in 2015 illustrates the asymmetric pain: the franc's 30% appreciation immediately devastated exporters (Swatch, Nestle, Roche) and the tourism industry, but it also dramatically lowered the cost of imported goods for Swiss consumers. The CHF rise was both blessing and curse — but the political pain came from the exporters, not from the consumers who benefitted silently.

13.2 The Balance of Payments: An Accounting Identity

📘 Principles Level — Every trade deficit is funded by a capital flow

One of the most elegant identities in macroeconomics says that, for any country, the trade balance must be offset by net capital flows. The intuition is straightforward. If Americans buy more goods from Japan than they sell to Japan, where do the extra yen come from? They come from selling dollar assets to Japanese investors (or borrowing from them). The country with a trade deficit is, by mathematical necessity, a country accumulating net liabilities to the rest of the world.

⚙ Equation Walkthrough — The Trade Balance / Capital Account Identity
NX = NCO (Trade Balance = Net Capital Outflow)
NX
Net exports (X − M). The current account balance (ignoring services and transfers, which we fold in here). Positive = trade surplus.
NCO
Net capital outflow: domestic purchases of foreign assets minus foreign purchases of domestic assets. Positive = country is lending abroad on net.
NX = NCO
An accounting identity. Not a behavioral claim. Forced by how the international accounts are constructed. A country running a trade surplus must be accumulating net foreign assets; a country running a trade deficit must be accumulating net foreign liabilities.

Reading it in plain language: goods flow one way; payment flows the other way. If Americans send fewer dollars worth of goods abroad than the dollar value of goods they receive (trade deficit), the difference is the value of American assets (Treasuries, stocks, real estate, debt) that foreigners are accumulating in exchange. The U.S. has run trade deficits for over 40 years, accumulating massive net foreign liabilities. This has been sustainable because the U.S. assets foreigners want to hold are large, deep, and trusted markets.

⚙ Equation Walkthrough — Saving, Investment, and the Trade Balance
S − I = NX = NCO
S
National saving (per chapter 10). Total income not consumed.
I
Domestic investment (real capital formation).
S − I
The country's excess saving over domestic investment opportunities. When S > I, the country invests the excess abroad (running a current account surplus). When S < I, the country borrows from abroad to fund investment (current account deficit).

The identity links chapter 10's loanable funds market to international flows. In a closed economy, S = I always. In an open economy, S need not equal I — the gap is exactly what flows across borders. The U.S. has S < I and runs a persistent trade deficit (importing capital to fund excess investment over saving). Germany has S > I and runs a persistent trade surplus (exporting capital because domestic investment opportunities are saturated). Trade imbalances don't happen because of "unfair trade practices" — they happen because of saving-investment imbalances between countries.

📐 Worked Example 13.1 — From saving-investment to the trade balance
An economy has the following: GDP = $25 trillion, C = $17T, I = $5T, G = $4.5T, T (net taxes) = $4T. (a) Compute private saving, public saving, and national saving. (b) Compute NX from the national accounts identity. (c) Verify that S − I = NX. (d) Comment on whether this country is a net borrower or lender to the rest of the world.
1
(a) Saving: Private = Y − T − C = 25 − 4 − 17 = $4T. Public = T − G = 4 − 4.5 = −$0.5T. National S = $4T + (−$0.5T) = $3.5T.
2
(b) NX from Y = C + I + G + NX: NX = Y − C − I − G = 25 − 17 − 5 − 4.5 = −$1.5T (trade deficit).
3
(c) Check S − I = NX: $3.5T − $5T = −$1.5T = NX ✓
4
(d) This country is a net borrower from the rest of the world. Domestic investment ($5T) exceeds national saving ($3.5T) by $1.5T; the gap is filled by net capital inflows from abroad. The trade deficit ($1.5T) is the goods-side mirror of the same capital flow.
The U.S. has fit this pattern for over four decades. Persistent trade deficits ($1-1.5T per year recently) are funded by persistent net capital inflows — foreigners buying U.S. Treasuries, corporate bonds, stocks, and real estate. This is not inherently bad: the U.S. uses the imported capital to fund productive investment, and U.S. residents benefit from foreign products and foreign-owned investment domestically. But the situation does create vulnerabilities (chapter 22 explores these in depth).

13.3 How Exchange Rates Are Determined

📘 Principles Level — The forex market as supply and demand

In the long run, exchange rates are determined by the relative purchasing power of currencies — a doctrine called purchasing power parity (PPP). In the short run, exchange rates are determined by the supply of and demand for currencies in foreign-exchange markets, with capital flows mattering far more than trade flows on a day-to-day basis. Both stories matter, on different time horizons.

⚙ Equation Walkthrough — Purchasing Power Parity
e = P / P* (in the long run, the exchange rate equals the price ratio)
e
Nominal exchange rate, quoted as units of foreign currency per one unit of domestic currency.
P
Domestic price level — the cost of a representative basket of goods at home.
P*
Foreign price level — the cost of the same basket abroad, in foreign currency.

Reading it in plain language: if the same basket costs $100 in the U.S. and 12,000 yen in Japan, then PPP says the exchange rate should be 120 yen per dollar (otherwise it would be cheaper to ship the basket from one country to the other and arbitrage the difference). PPP works reasonably well at long horizons (decades) but fails substantially in the short run, because many goods cannot be cheaply arbitraged (haircuts, hospital stays, real estate), and because exchange rates are also driven by capital flows that have nothing to do with goods prices.

The Economist's Big Mac Index tests PPP using the simplest possible "basket": the price of a Big Mac in different countries. As of 2025, a Big Mac costs $5.79 in the U.S. and roughly 25 yuan in China. PPP would imply an exchange rate of about 4.3 yuan/USD. The actual market rate is around 7.2 yuan/USD — meaning the yuan trades about 40% below its PPP value. The Big Mac Index is whimsical but conceptually revealing: persistent deviations from PPP suggest persistent over- or undervaluation of currencies relative to their fundamentals.

🎛 Interactive — The Foreign Exchange Market

Drag the sliders; watch the equilibrium exchange rate adjust

What you're seeing: the blue line is the supply of CHF (Swiss francs) to the forex market — slopes up because at higher CHF prices, more Swiss residents want to sell. The red line is the demand for CHF — slopes down because at higher CHF prices, fewer foreigners can afford Swiss exports and assets. The gray dashed lines show the original curves. Green dot = new equilibrium.

Try: Set "Foreign capital inflow" to +20 (safe-haven inflows like 2011-2015). Watch CHF appreciate sharply. Now add "Central bank intervention" of −20 (SNB printing CHF to buy euros, keeping the rate down) — this is what the SNB did for three years. Then set intervention to 0 — that's January 15, 2015. The currency snaps to its market-clearing level.

13.4 The Switzerland 2015 Case: Every Concept in One Event

2011–2015 Switzerland Currency intervention

The Day the SNB Surrendered: 90 Seconds That Reshaped Forex Markets

Between 2010 and 2011, the European debt crisis sent terrified capital fleeing the Eurozone in search of safe havens. Switzerland — small, politically stable, with a long tradition of monetary discipline and bank secrecy — became the destination of choice for trillions of euros of nervous money. By August 2011, the Swiss franc had appreciated nearly 40% against the euro in just over a year, threatening to crush Swiss exporters and tourism. Watch makers, pharmaceutical companies, machinery exporters all complained that their products had become uncompetitive overnight. Deflation was looming. The Swiss National Bank had to act.

On September 6, 2011, the SNB announced an unprecedented commitment: it would buy foreign currency in unlimited quantities to prevent the franc from appreciating beyond 1.20 per euro. This was not a guideline or a target — it was a hard floor. The SNB pledged to print as many francs as necessary, swap them for euros at 1.20, and absorb the resulting balance-sheet expansion. For three and a half years, the policy worked. The franc stayed close to 1.20. Swiss exports recovered. Tourism stabilized.

But the policy had costs. To defend the floor, the SNB had to keep buying euros — and the inflows kept coming. By December 2014, the SNB held foreign currency reserves of roughly 500 billion CHF, equivalent to about 70% of Swiss GDP. To put that in perspective: imagine the Fed accumulating $20 trillion of foreign-currency reserves on its balance sheet, far exceeding U.S. annual output. The SNB's balance sheet had become a massive bet that the euro would not depreciate further. And then, in early January 2015, signals from the European Central Bank suggested that a massive new round of euro-weakening quantitative easing was imminent.

The SNB faced two options: (1) continue defending the floor and watch its balance sheet grow indefinitely as Mario Draghi's ECB flooded the market with euros, eventually owning much of the eurozone's outstanding government debt; or (2) abandon the floor and accept a one-time massive franc appreciation. They chose option 2. On January 15, 2015 at 9:30 GMT, the floor was removed. Within ninety seconds the franc was up 30% — briefly trading as high as 0.85 CHF per euro before settling around 1.04. Simultaneously, the SNB cut the deposit rate to −0.75%, the lowest in history, to discourage further capital inflows.

The damage was widespread. Several forex brokers, including FXCM, Alpari UK, and Excel Markets, became insolvent. Citibank, Deutsche Bank, and Barclays reported losses of $150-300 million each. Hedge funds with short-CHF positions were wiped out. Polish and Hungarian homeowners with Swiss-franc mortgages saw their effective debts jump 20-30% overnight. The Swiss stock market fell more than 10% in a single day. Swatch's CEO called it "a tsunami."

The longer-term consequences were more nuanced. Swiss exports did suffer initially (about 1-2% of GDP impact), but the country avoided the Japan-style stagnation many had feared. Swiss companies cut costs, became more efficient, and pivoted toward higher-value products. By 2018, the franc had retreated somewhat toward 1.15-1.18 EUR/CHF. The SNB's reserves stabilized around 95% of GDP — extraordinary, but no longer growing.

What this case teaches: A small open economy faced with massive capital inflows has only bad options. Intervention can defer the problem but accumulates fiscal risk (a giant central bank balance sheet bet against the home currency). Abandoning intervention triggers immediate trauma. There is no clean path. The single most important lesson is that currency intervention is profoundly asymmetric: easy to start, agonizing to stop, dangerous to maintain. This is exactly why most major central banks have moved away from explicit pegs and toward floating regimes with implicit guardrails.

Stop and reflect: The SNB's three-and-a-half-year defense of the 1.20 floor accumulated foreign currency reserves equal to about 70% of Swiss GDP. If Swiss inflation had been low and ECB-style quantitative easing had not been expected, the SNB could have continued defending the floor indefinitely. Why didn't this option remain feasible? Think about what happens when a central bank holds enormous foreign assets denominated in a currency the foreign country's own central bank is trying to weaken.

13.5 The Impossible Trinity (Trilemma): Three Things, Pick Two

📘 Principles Level — The hardest constraint in international finance

Every open economy faces a fundamental constraint articulated by Robert Mundell in the 1960s. A country can pursue at most two of the following three desirable policies — never all three at once:

  1. A fixed exchange rate — a peg that lets businesses plan and protects against currency volatility.
  2. Free movement of capital — letting investment flow in and out without restrictions, which most modern economies value.
  3. An independent monetary policy — being able to set interest rates to fight domestic recession or inflation.

This is the impossible trinity or trilemma. It is one of the most rigorously tested empirical regularities in macroeconomics, and it has shaped every major international monetary architecture from Bretton Woods (1944-1971) to the European Monetary Union to modern emerging-market debates over capital controls.

🎛 Interactive — The Trilemma Triangle

Click a corner to choose your country's policy combination

What you're seeing: the three corners of the triangle are the three desirable policies. The colored side connecting two corners represents the policy combination a country chooses; the opposite corner is what gets sacrificed. There is no real-world economy that operates at the center — the trilemma forces a choice.

The trilemma in practice — how real countries make the trade-off
Country / regimeCombination chosenCost of the sacrificed policy
Eurozone members Fixed exchange rate (irrevocably, by adopting the euro) + free capital flows No independent monetary policy. Greece can't devalue or cut its own interest rates during recessions. This was the central pain of the 2010-2012 Greek crisis.
Hong Kong Fixed rate to USD (HKD pegged since 1983) + open capital markets Hong Kong's interest rates effectively mirror U.S. interest rates. When the Fed raises rates, Hong Kong must raise too, even if its local economy doesn't warrant it.
China (pre-2015) Fixed (pegged) rate + independent monetary policy Heavy capital controls. Chinese citizens couldn't move money abroad without restrictions; foreign investors couldn't freely enter Chinese asset markets.
U.S., UK, Japan, EU vs. world Free capital flows + independent monetary policy Floating exchange rate. The dollar, pound, yen, and euro all swing significantly in response to shocks.
Switzerland 2011–2015 Tried for all three! Floor on the franc + capital openness + ability to cut rates The trilemma caught up with them. By 2015, defending the floor against ECB easing required printing infinite francs. They had to abandon either the floor (which they did) or capital openness (politically unacceptable).
✦ Sharma's Take — Why the trilemma is the most useful tool you'll learn in this chapter

The trilemma is what separates students who understand international macroeconomics from those who don't. Anyone can recite the equations of PPP and the balance of payments. The trilemma is harder because it forces you to think about which constraint binds in a given situation.

When you read a news article about a currency crisis, an emerging market in trouble, a central bank facing speculative pressure — always ask: "Which corner of the trilemma are they trying to live at? Why is it impossible to remain there? Which corner are they being forced toward?" Argentina in 2001, Mexico in 1994, Thailand in 1997, Britain in 1992, Russia in 1998, Switzerland in 2015 — every one of these crises was fundamentally a trilemma collision. Capital flows changed; the country could no longer simultaneously hold a peg, open capital markets, and an independent policy. Something had to give.

The trilemma also explains why the eurozone has been such a difficult experiment. Member countries gave up their independent monetary policy in exchange for the certainty of a fixed rate and open capital markets within the bloc. When asymmetric shocks hit (like the 2009-2012 sovereign debt crisis affecting Greece more than Germany), countries on the wrong end had no independent monetary policy to deploy. The solution had to come from fiscal transfers (which Germany resisted) or from the European Central Bank (which broke with its formal mandate to act). The trilemma was the deep constraint shaping every policy decision in that crisis.

— A.S.

13.6 Exchange Rate Regimes: A Spectrum of Choices

Exchange rate regimes from rigid to flexible
RegimeWhat it isExamples / costs
Dollarization / Currency Union Country abandons its own currency entirely; adopts a foreign currency or shares one with neighbors. Ecuador (USD since 2000), El Salvador (USD since 2001), Eurozone members. Permanent loss of monetary policy. Permanent gain of credibility.
Hard peg (currency board) Domestic currency exists but is rigidly tied to a foreign anchor, with full reserve backing. Hong Kong dollar (1983-present). Bulgaria's lev. Easier to abandon than dollarization but politically costly.
Soft peg / adjustable peg Official target rate that can be changed in extremis. Central bank intervenes to defend it. Pre-1971 Bretton Woods system. Modern: Saudi Arabia (riyal/USD), most Gulf states. Vulnerable to speculative attack when fundamentals diverge.
Managed float Rate floats day-to-day but central bank intervenes at extremes or against destabilizing speculation. China (post-2015), India, Brazil. The most common modern regime for emerging markets.
Free float Exchange rate determined purely by markets. Central bank does not intervene. U.S., UK, Japan, Eurozone (vs. outside world), Australia, Canada. Volatility but maximum monetary independence.

13.7 Why Trade Deficits Are Mostly Misunderstood

📘 Principles Level — A trade deficit is not a national debt

"Trade deficit" is one of the most consistently misused terms in political discourse. Treated as a kind of national scoreboard, it is invoked to justify tariffs, sanctions, and protectionist policy. Most of the rhetoric is wrong. A trade deficit is neither inherently bad nor inherently good — it depends on what's funding it and what the imported capital is being used for.

From section 13.2, recall: S − I = NX. A trade deficit (NX < 0) means national saving falls short of domestic investment by an amount equal to the deficit. This can happen for two very different reasons:

  1. Investment is unusually high (productive capital formation outpacing domestic saving). Foreign capital flows in to fund profitable opportunities. Examples: U.S. in the 1990s tech boom; Korea during its industrialization push. This kind of trade deficit is healthy — the foreign capital pays for future productive capacity.
  2. Saving is unusually low (consumption outpacing income). Foreign capital flows in to fund current consumption. Examples: Greece pre-2010 (importing German Mercedes and Italian wine on credit); U.S. mortgage borrowing 2003-2007. This kind of trade deficit is problematic — debts accumulate without productive capacity to service them.

The trade deficit by itself doesn't tell you which case you're in. You need to look at what investment is being funded and whether it has decent returns.

⚠ Common Pitfall — "Trade deficits transfer jobs abroad"

A widespread political claim. It contains a kernel of truth — when Americans buy Chinese goods instead of American ones, some specific U.S. workers (in textiles, manufacturing) lose jobs. But the aggregate-level claim is largely wrong. The U.S. trade deficit has fluctuated between 3% and 6% of GDP for forty years, while U.S. unemployment has ranged from 3.5% to 10% over the same period — the two have essentially no correlation. Aggregate U.S. employment is determined by domestic factors (Fed policy, fiscal stance, structural conditions), not by the trade balance.

This doesn't mean trade has no distributional effects: it does. Specific workers and regions lose; others gain. The mistake is treating the trade balance as the aggregate scoreboard rather than understanding it as a financial-flow consequence of saving and investment decisions. Tariffs that try to "reduce the trade deficit" almost always fail to do so, because they don't change underlying saving-investment imbalances; they just shift which goods get imported.

🎮 MacroNations Connection — The Trilemma in the Simulation

In MacroNations, your Exchange Rate Regime lever lets you choose your trilemma combination: float (free capital + independent policy), peg (fixed + capital free, no independent policy), or managed (in-between). Your Capital Account Openness setting determines whether your country accepts free flows or imposes controls. Your Interest Rate is constrained by your regime choice — if you peg, your rate is forced to track your anchor country; if you float with controls, you can set rates more freely.

The Switzerland 2015-style scenario can play out in the simulation: pegging during a flight to safety triggers massive reserve accumulation, which can become unsustainable. Choosing to abandon the peg at the wrong moment costs Stability points (a one-time hit) but releases pressure. The simulation rewards strategies that match the regime to the country's economic structure: small open economies pegged to large stable trading partners benefit from low volatility but accept the discipline; large economies typically gain more from floating.

U.S. Trade-Weighted Dollar Index (Broad)
Source: Federal Reserve via FRED (Series: DTWEXBGS). The trade-weighted dollar index aggregates the dollar's value against a basket of U.S. trading partners' currencies, weighted by trade volumes. Note three big cycles: the early-1980s Volcker disinflation pushed the dollar up sharply; the late-1990s tech boom and 2014-2015 commodity collapse pushed it up again; the post-2020 period saw both inflation-driven weakness and rate-driven strength. Dollar movements of 10-20% can substantially affect U.S. inflation, exports, and emerging-market debt burdens.
U.S. Net Exports (% of GDP)
Source: BEA via FRED (Series: NETEXP, divided by GDP). The U.S. has run persistent trade deficits since the early 1980s, averaging around −3% of GDP, with the deficit widening to about −5% pre-2008 and narrowing during the GFC. This persistent deficit is the mirror of persistent net capital inflows — foreigners' demand for U.S. assets has consistently exceeded U.S. residents' demand for foreign assets, which keeps the dollar relatively strong and imports relatively cheap.

Chapter 13 Quiz

Trade, capital flows, exchange rates, trilemma
Part A — Multiple Choice (Principles)
1. A country running a trade deficit of $500 billion must, by accounting identity, have:
A Net capital outflows of $500 billion (residents lending to the rest of the world)
B Net capital inflows of $500 billion (foreigners lending to the country)
C Net exports of $500 billion
D Zero capital flows
2. Purchasing power parity (PPP) implies that:
A Exchange rates are determined exclusively by central bank intervention
B In the long run, exchange rates adjust to equalize the cost of representative baskets of goods across countries
C Inflation rates must be the same in all countries
D Trade deficits are always corrected by exchange rate movements
3. The Swiss National Bank's January 15, 2015 decision had what immediate effect on the Swiss franc?
A Depreciated about 30% against the euro
B Appreciated about 30% against the euro
C No change in value
D Switched from convertible to inconvertible
4. The "impossible trinity" or trilemma states that an open economy cannot simultaneously have:
A Low inflation, low unemployment, and high growth
B A fixed exchange rate, free capital flows, and an independent monetary policy
C Free trade, balanced budgets, and price stability
D Open borders, fiscal sovereignty, and a national currency
Part B — Multiple Choice (Intermediate)
5. A Eurozone member country experiences a deep recession while Germany's economy is overheating. Under the trilemma, what tool is NOT available to the recession-hit country?
A Fiscal stimulus through tax cuts
B Government spending increases
C Currency devaluation to boost exports
D Labor market reforms
6. The U.S. has run persistent trade deficits for over 40 years. The corresponding capital-account counterpart is:
A Persistent net capital inflows from foreigners buying U.S. assets
B Persistent depletion of U.S. foreign reserves
C Persistent surpluses on the U.S. fiscal account
D No corresponding capital flow — the identity does not apply to large countries
7. Hong Kong has maintained a hard peg of the HKD to the USD since 1983. According to the trilemma, Hong Kong has sacrificed:
A Free capital flows
B A fixed exchange rate
C An independent monetary policy
D Free trade in goods
Part C — Numerical Problems
8. Country A's price level is 100 (in local currency); Country B's price level is 120 (in its local currency). The nominal exchange rate is 0.85 (units of B's currency per unit of A's currency). (a) Compute the real exchange rate, expressed as units of B's goods per unit of A's goods. (b) Is country A's currency overvalued or undervalued relative to PPP? By how much? (c) What would you expect to happen to the nominal rate in the long run if PPP holds?
Model answer: (a) Real exchange rate ε = e × P / P* = 0.85 × 100 / 120 = 0.708. A unit of A's goods (when sold in B) is worth only 0.708 units of B's goods — A's goods are CHEAP relative to B's after accounting for the exchange rate. (b) PPP rate would be e = P*/P (rearranged to express foreign-per-domestic) = 120/100 = 1.20. Actual rate is 0.85, so A's currency is undervalued by about 29%: (1.20 − 0.85) / 1.20. (c) Long-run PPP predicts the nominal rate will rise from 0.85 toward 1.20 — A's currency will appreciate by about 41% to restore parity, OR A's prices will rise faster than B's, OR some combination. The Big Mac Index regularly identifies such divergences for currencies like the yuan, Indian rupee, and Argentine peso.
9. A small economy has GDP = $300B, C = $200B, I = $80B, G = $50B. (a) Compute NX directly from the national accounts identity. (b) The country also reports private saving of $60B and public saving of −$10B. Compute national saving. (c) Verify S − I = NX. (d) Is this country a net borrower or lender to the rest of the world?
Model answer: (a) NX = Y − C − I − G = 300 − 200 − 80 − 50 = −$30B (trade deficit). (b) National saving S = $60B + (−$10B) = $50B. (c) S − I = $50B − $80B = −$30B = NX ✓. (d) Net borrower: investment exceeds saving by $30B, financed by net capital inflow of $30B. The trade deficit ($30B) and capital inflow ($30B) are two sides of the same accounting coin.
Part D — Policy Scenario
Scenario: A small open economy has pegged its currency to the euro for 8 years and enjoyed low inflation and stable trade with the EU. Now a regional crisis triggers massive capital inflows seeking safety. The central bank has accumulated foreign reserves equal to 50% of GDP defending the peg. The European Central Bank is signaling more easing, which will weaken the euro and require the small country's central bank to print even more local currency to defend the peg. Domestic inflation is starting to rise. Three options: (1) maintain the peg and accept rising inflation and balance-sheet risk; (2) abandon the peg and accept currency appreciation; (3) impose temporary capital controls to slow inflows while reassessing.
10. Write a 250-word memo for the Finance Minister. Reference: the trilemma, the Swiss 2015 precedent, the costs and benefits of each option, and the political-economy dimensions. Take a clear position.
Model answer (sketch): The country has run into the trilemma. With open capital markets and a euro peg, monetary policy is constrained — the only way to defend the peg is unlimited reserve accumulation. Option 1 (maintain peg) defers the problem but worsens it. The 2015 Swiss precedent shows that defending a peg against persistent appreciation pressure leads to a balance sheet that eventually becomes politically unsustainable. Rising domestic inflation also signals the peg has become incompatible with domestic objectives. Option 2 (abandon peg) imposes immediate trauma — currency appreciation, exporter losses, possible recession — but releases the trilemma constraint. Switzerland survived the 2015 shock; the economy adapted within 2-3 years. The pain is concentrated and front-loaded. Option 3 (capital controls) shifts the trilemma sacrifice from monetary independence to capital openness. Temporary controls can buy time but signal regime instability and may trigger preemptive capital flight. Position: Adopt option 2 (abandon peg), but combine it with mitigating measures — temporary fiscal support for affected exporters, a moderate negative interest rate to dampen the appreciation, and a clear communication strategy framing the move as a chosen transition rather than a forced retreat. Do not maintain the peg. The Swiss case shows the longer you defend, the more catastrophic the eventual unwind. The trilemma is unforgiving, and the policy that works is to choose your sacrifice deliberately rather than have it imposed on you by markets.
This is a preview — the live textbook auto-grades MCQs and stores progress.

Chapter 14 · Part VII: Short-Run Economic Fluctuations

Unemployment

How we measure who is out of work, why some unemployment never disappears, why the 2020-2024 U.S. labor market broke every textbook model — and why the most important diagram in modern labor economics rarely appears in introductory texts

On April 1, 1992, New Jersey raised its minimum wage from $4.25 to $5.05 per hour — about a 19% increase in one shot. According to every economics textbook in print at the time, this should have destroyed jobs. The standard supply-and-demand diagram showed it clearly: set a price floor above the market clearing wage, and quantity demanded falls below quantity supplied. Fast-food restaurants employing low-wage workers should have cut staff, raised prices, or closed. Two Princeton economists — David Card and Alan Krueger — decided to find out whether the standard prediction was actually right. They surveyed 410 fast-food restaurants in New Jersey and just across the border in eastern Pennsylvania, where the minimum wage stayed at $4.25. They counted employees before the increase and after.

The result published in the American Economic Review in 1994 stunned the profession. Fast-food employment in New Jersey, where wages had been forced up, actually rose by about 13% relative to Pennsylvania. The textbook prediction was not just wrong in magnitude — it had the wrong sign. David Card would eventually win the 2021 Nobel Prize in Economics for this and related work, which permanently changed how economists think about labor markets. The simple supply-and-demand model that you learned in Chapter 4 of every microeconomics textbook turns out to be a poor description of how most labor markets actually function.

Twenty-eight years later, in April 2020, the U.S. economy demonstrated the opposite extreme. In a single month, U.S. unemployment jumped from 3.5% to 14.8% — the fastest, sharpest spike in the history of the data series, which goes back to 1948. Twenty-two million Americans lost their jobs in eight weeks. And then, even more remarkably, by January 2023 — less than three years later — unemployment had fallen to 3.4%, the lowest level since 1969. The labor market had recovered with a speed nobody believed was possible. By April 2023, the unemployment rate had drifted up modestly, and by early 2026 it sat around 4.3-4.4%. The 2020-2024 period was the most violently nonlinear stretch of U.S. labor-market history since the Great Depression.

This chapter is about unemployment: how it is measured, why it never reaches zero (and shouldn't), what causes it to spike during recessions, and how to think about the labor market when the simple textbook model so visibly fails. The most important new tool we introduce — one that older textbooks barely cover — is the Beveridge curve, the relationship between unemployment and job openings. The Beveridge curve is what economists actually use to read the modern labor market, and the dramatic shifts in this curve during 2020-2024 tell the story of the most consequential labor-market episode of our lifetimes.

✦ Sharma's Take

Unemployment is the macroeconomic variable people feel most viscerally. Inflation eats away at savings slowly; GDP growth is an abstraction. But losing your job is immediate, personal, and devastating. The labor market is also the macroeconomic arena where simple textbook models fail most spectacularly. The standard "wage equals marginal product of labor" framework predicts that minimum wages destroy jobs and that wages adjust quickly to clear markets. The empirical evidence on both points is, charitably, mixed — and on the minimum wage front, frequently contradictory to the standard prediction.

Why is this? Because labor markets are not like wheat markets. Workers and jobs are heterogeneous; matching is costly; firms care about morale and turnover; long-term relationships matter; and information about who is good at what is genuinely scarce. Modern labor economics — the kind that earned David Card his Nobel — treats these frictions as the first-order features, not the second-order corrections, of the labor market. This chapter is built around that newer view. If at the end of the chapter you can read a news article about the labor market and not be fooled by sloppy supply-and-demand reasoning, I will have done my job.

— A.S.

14.1 How We Measure Unemployment

📘 Principles Level — The categories everyone in macroeconomics uses

Every month, the Bureau of Labor Statistics (BLS) surveys about 60,000 U.S. households as part of the Current Population Survey. Based on what people answer, each adult (age 16+) gets placed into exactly one of three categories:

  1. Employed: did any paid work in the past week, even one hour. Includes part-time and self-employed workers.
  2. Unemployed: did no paid work but actively searched for a job in the past four weeks AND is available to start work.
  3. Not in the labor force: not working and not searching — students, retirees, stay-at-home parents, discouraged workers who have given up, the disabled.

These three categories sum to the total adult population. The first two add up to the labor force — people either working or looking for work.

⚙ Equation Walkthrough — The Standard Unemployment Rate (U-3)
u = Unemployed / Labor Force × 100%
u
The unemployment rate, reported monthly as a percentage. This is the headline number you see in the news. The BLS officially calls it U-3.
Unemployed
People without jobs who have actively searched in the past 4 weeks. People who have given up looking ("discouraged workers") are NOT counted as unemployed — they fall into "not in the labor force." This is a crucial measurement quirk.
Labor Force
Employed + Unemployed. Does NOT include people who are neither working nor looking.

In March 2026 (most recent month at writing): Labor force = 170.09 million. Unemployed = 7.24 million. u = 7.24 / 170.09 = 4.26%, which the BLS reports as 4.3%.

⚙ Equation Walkthrough — Two Other Important Measures
LFPR = Labor Force / Adult Population × 100%
E/P = Employed / Adult Population × 100%
LFPR
Labor force participation rate. What share of adults are either working or looking? In the U.S. this was around 67% in 2000, fell to 63% after the Great Recession, dropped to 60.2% in April 2020 (lowest since the early 1970s), and as of early 2026 sits around 61.9%. This drop is consequential: about 5 million Americans have left the labor force since 2000 who would have been in it under prior trends.
E/P
Employment-to-population ratio. Sidesteps the search-effort issue by just asking: what share of adults actually have jobs? In April 2023, when U-3 hit its 50-year low of 3.4%, E/P was 60.4% — meaning nearly 40% of working-age adults were not employed, even at "full employment." This is the gap LFPR tries to highlight.
⚠ Common Pitfall — "Unemployment fell, so the job market is great"

A widely-reported and widely-misunderstood phenomenon. The unemployment rate can fall for two very different reasons:

  1. Good reason: people who were unemployed found jobs. Both employment and labor force participation rise.
  2. Bad reason: people who were unemployed gave up looking. They drop out of the labor force entirely. Unemployment falls because the denominator shrinks, not because more people are working.

To distinguish these, always look at three numbers together: unemployment rate, labor force participation rate, AND employment-to-population ratio. In a healthy recovery, all three move in healthy directions. In a discouragement-driven decline, unemployment looks good but LFPR and E/P both deteriorate. This is why economists obsess over labor force participation — it strips away the measurement artifact.

📚 The Alternative Unemployment Rates — U-4, U-5, U-6
U-3
The headline rate. Unemployed who actively searched ÷ labor force. ~4.3% in March 2026.
U-4
U-3 plus discouraged workers (gave up looking because they think no jobs are available).
U-5
U-4 plus other marginally attached workers (would like a job but haven't searched recently for various reasons).
U-6
U-5 plus part-time workers who want full-time work but can't get it (involuntary part-time). The most comprehensive measure. ~8.0% in March 2026.

Why so many measures? Because "unemployed" is a fuzzy category. A laid-off engineer searching online every day is unemployed; a parent who wants to work but has stopped looking because childcare is impossible is "not in the labor force"; a barista working 15 hours a week who wants 40 is "employed." Each of these has different policy implications, so the BLS publishes a spectrum of measures. The gap between U-3 and U-6 tells you how much "hidden slack" there is in the labor market.

📐 Worked Example 14.1 — Reading a real labor market snapshot
In March 2026, the BLS reported: Adult population (16+) = 274.7 million. Labor force = 170.09 million. Employed = 162.85 million. Unemployed = 7.24 million. (a) Verify the unemployment rate. (b) Compute the labor force participation rate. (c) Compute the employment-to-population ratio. (d) If 5 million people who were "not in the labor force" all started looking for jobs tomorrow but didn't find them, what would the unemployment rate become?
1
(a) Unemployment rate: u = 7.24 / 170.09 = 4.256% ≈ 4.3% ✓ matches the BLS report.
2
(b) LFPR: 170.09 / 274.7 = 61.92%. Two-thirds of a percentage point below the pre-COVID level of 63.4% in February 2020.
3
(c) E/P ratio: 162.85 / 274.7 = 59.28%. Even at low headline unemployment, fewer than 60% of adults have jobs.
4
(d) Counterfactual: labor force rises to 175.09 million, unemployed rises to 12.24 million. u = 12.24 / 175.09 = 6.99% ≈ 7%. The "true" slack in the economy depends heavily on how many discouraged workers re-enter the labor force as conditions improve.
This is exactly what happens during recoveries. As job prospects improve, people who had given up start looking again. The headline unemployment rate can stay flat or even rise during a recovery's early months, because the inflow of returning workers temporarily exceeds the rate at which they find jobs. This is one of the main reasons why a falling unemployment rate during the early months of a recovery is sometimes a worse sign than a stable one — it can mean people are giving up, not that jobs are appearing.

14.2 The Three Types of Unemployment

📘 Principles Level — Why unemployment never goes to zero

Even in the best of economic times, the U.S. unemployment rate has never been below about 2.5%. Why doesn't it go to zero? Because some unemployment is structural to how labor markets work — it never disappears, even in booms. Economists divide unemployment into three categories based on what's causing it:

📚 The Three Types of Unemployment
Frictional
Short-term unemployment as workers move between jobs. A recent graduate looking for their first job; an engineer who quit to find a better fit; a parent re-entering the workforce after raising children. This is unavoidable in any economy where people move, change careers, or seek better matches. Roughly 1-2% of unemployment is frictional in good times.
Structural
Long-term unemployment caused by a mismatch between worker skills/location and job requirements. A coal miner in West Virginia when coal jobs disappear; a steelworker in 1980s Ohio when domestic steel collapses; a journalist in 2010s when local newspapers fail. The skills and the locations don't match the new economy. Structural unemployment can persist for years; the unemployed worker may need retraining or relocation to find work.
Cyclical
Unemployment caused by recessions — insufficient overall demand for goods and services. When consumers stop spending, businesses lay off workers regardless of their skill or location. This is the kind of unemployment we'll address with monetary and fiscal policy in Chapters 17-19. Cyclical unemployment can vanish quickly once demand recovers.
⚙ The Natural Rate of Unemployment
u = u* + cyclical component
where u* = natural rate (frictional + structural)
u*
Natural rate of unemployment — the unemployment rate that prevails when the economy is at potential output. NOT zero. Reflects the unavoidable churn of frictional and structural unemployment. In the U.S. today, most estimates put u* somewhere between 3.5% and 4.5%.
u − u*
Cyclical unemployment — the gap between current and natural rates. Positive in recessions, negative in booms (when demand exceeds normal and the economy "overheats"). This is the part that monetary policy can affect.

Why u* is invisible: the natural rate is never directly observed; it must be estimated from data, often with substantial uncertainty. The CBO, Fed, and private forecasters publish estimates of u* that frequently differ by a percentage point or more. Knowing u* is essential for monetary policy — the Fed tightens when actual unemployment is below u* and loosens when above — but the uncertainty is exactly why monetary policy is so difficult.

14.3 The Beveridge Curve: The Most Important Diagram in Modern Labor Economics

📘 Principles Level — Job openings versus unemployment

Imagine you plot two things over time. On the horizontal axis, the unemployment rate. On the vertical axis, the job openings rate — the number of unfilled jobs as a share of total employment plus openings. You'd expect these to be negatively related: when unemployment is high, there are few openings (recessions), and when unemployment is low, openings are abundant (booms). And indeed, that's exactly what the data show.

This downward-sloping relationship is called the Beveridge curve, named after Lord William Beveridge, the British economist who first identified it in the 1940s. The Beveridge curve is the closest thing modern labor economics has to a fundamental diagram. Every recession traces a movement along the curve. But sometimes — and this is the interesting part — the entire curve shifts. When the curve shifts outward (up and to the right), it means the economy is getting less efficient at matching unemployed workers to job openings. When it shifts inward, matching is getting better.

⚙ The Beveridge Curve in Words
Vacancy Rate = f(Unemployment Rate)
with slope < 0 and curvature reflecting matching frictions
v
Vacancy rate: job openings as a share of (employment + openings). U.S. averages about 4-5% in normal times; spiked to a record 7% in 2022.
u
Unemployment rate: as defined in §14.1.
Movements along
Cyclical changes in labor demand. Recession → up-right movement (high u, low v). Boom → down-left movement (low u, high v).
Shifts of
Changes in matching efficiency — how easily firms and workers find each other. Outward shift = bad matching (more u for any v). Inward shift = good matching.

Why this matters: the Beveridge curve gives policymakers a way to distinguish between cyclical unemployment (movements along the curve, fixable with monetary policy) and structural unemployment (shifts of the curve, requiring training, relocation help, or time). The Fed cares enormously about which one is dominant at any moment.

14.4 The 2020-2024 U.S. Labor Market: Three Beveridge Curve Shifts in Four Years

2020–2024 United States Labor market

The Most Violent Beveridge Curve Episode in U.S. History

The pre-COVID U.S. Beveridge curve, traced out over 2010-2019, was a well-behaved downward-sloping arc. Unemployment fell from 9.6% (2010) to 3.5% (Feb 2020); the vacancy rate rose from about 2% to 4.5%. The curve was reasonably stable, with mild inward improvement as the recovery matured. Then came the pandemic.

Phase 1 — March-April 2020: explosive outward shift. In two months, U.S. unemployment exploded from 3.5% to 14.8%. Twenty-two million jobs vanished. But job openings didn't fall proportionally; many firms still had open postings they couldn't fill because of lockdowns. For the first time in modern data, the U.S. had simultaneously high unemployment AND not-low vacancies. The Beveridge curve had shifted dramatically outward — matching efficiency had collapsed.

Phase 2 — Summer 2020: partial reversal. As lockdowns eased, many of the workers laid off in March-April were recalled by their previous employers. This created an unusually high "matching efficiency" period — workers and jobs reconnected because they already had pre-existing relationships. Unemployment fell to 6.7% by November 2020 without a major rise in job openings. The Beveridge curve traced back partway toward its pre-pandemic position.

Phase 3 — 2021-2022: dramatic outward shift again. This is the most interesting and unusual part of the story. Starting in early 2021, job openings began climbing to unprecedented levels, eventually peaking at 12.0 million openings in March 2022 (a vacancy rate of ~7%). At the same time, unemployment came down only modestly — sitting at 3.6% by March 2022, similar to pre-pandemic levels but with the labor force still 2 million people smaller. The Beveridge curve had shifted dramatically outward and upward: there were 2 job openings for every unemployed worker, an all-time record.

Why did matching efficiency break down so severely? Three reasons economists generally point to:

  • The Great Resignation — record-high quit rates as employed workers reevaluated careers, retired early, or switched industries. This made matching harder because firms were competing for workers who were themselves looking, not just for unemployed workers searching the same firms.
  • Geographic mismatch — jobs were in growing tech and logistics sectors; many unemployed had service-sector skills.
  • Demographic shifts — early retirement during the pandemic permanently removed millions from the labor force.

Phase 4 — 2023-2025: gradual normalization. By late 2023, job openings had eased to 8-9 million and the unemployment rate had drifted up from its 3.4% bottom toward 4%. The labor market was cooling without a recession. By early 2026, unemployment sat at 4.3% with vacancies around 7-8 million — still slightly above the pre-pandemic Beveridge curve but no longer in record-anomaly territory.

The four-year journey is the most remarkable Beveridge-curve episode ever recorded. It illustrates that the labor market can break and re-form in unexpected ways, and that the relationship between unemployment and vacancies — far from being a fixed economic law — is itself an outcome of social, demographic, and structural dynamics.

🎛 Interactive — The Beveridge Curve

Trace the U.S. labor market through four years; see the shifts

What you're seeing: the unemployment rate (X) and the job vacancy rate (Y), with each phase highlighted. The pre-COVID Beveridge curve (gray dots) represents normal labor market matching. When the curve shifts up-and-right, matching efficiency has worsened. The dramatic 2021-22 shift represents the Great Resignation period — record openings AND not-low unemployment, which violated everything textbooks taught.

14.5 Why Wages Don't Fully Adjust: Four Theories

📘 Principles Level — Why labor markets don't clear like wheat markets

The simple supply-and-demand model predicts that when unemployment rises, wages fall, which restores full employment. In practice, wages are remarkably sticky downward: they almost never fall in nominal terms even during severe recessions. During 2008-09, with unemployment reaching 10%, U.S. nominal wages continued rising at about 2% per year. Why don't wages adjust the way price theory predicts they should?

Modern labor economics identifies four major reasons.

Why wages stay high in the face of unemployment — four theories
TheoryThe mechanismReal-world evidence
Minimum wages Legal floors below which wages cannot fall. Binding mostly for low-skill workers. U.S. federal minimum is $7.25/hour (unchanged since 2009), but many states are well above (CA $16+). About 1% of hourly workers earn at or below the federal floor.
Unions and collective bargaining Workers organize and negotiate wages above the competitive level, with credible threats to strike. U.S. union membership has fallen from ~33% in 1955 to ~10% in 2026, mostly in the public sector. In Europe, coverage is much higher (60%+ in Belgium, France, Italy).
Efficiency wages Firms pay above the market-clearing wage on purpose, because higher wages improve worker effort, reduce turnover, attract better candidates, and boost loyalty. (Henry Ford famously did this in 1914 with the $5/day wage.) Strongly supported by empirical evidence. Studies show that firms paying above-market wages have lower turnover, higher productivity, and stronger applicant pools — often a net financial benefit.
Insider-outsider theory Already-employed workers ("insiders") have bargaining power that the unemployed ("outsiders") lack. Insiders negotiate to protect their wages even when outsiders would gladly work for less. Why a recession can persist: firms could lower wages and hire more, but insider workers resist, the unemployed can't bid wages down, and the system stalls at high unemployment.
1992–2021 New Jersey vs. Pennsylvania Card-Krueger

The Card-Krueger Minimum Wage Study That Broke the Standard Model

The standard textbook treatment of minimum wages is unambiguous. A minimum wage set above the market-clearing wage creates a surplus — quantity supplied exceeds quantity demanded — which means unemployment for low-wage workers. The diagram is the first thing taught in every introductory micro course. The empirical evidence used to back it up: aggregate statistical comparisons of teenage employment rates across states with different minimum wages.

In 1992, David Card and Alan Krueger of Princeton University set out to test the prediction directly using a quasi-experiment. New Jersey raised its minimum wage from $4.25 to $5.05 per hour on April 1, 1992 — a 19% increase. Pennsylvania, just across the border, kept its minimum at $4.25. The two states had similar economies and similar fast-food industries. If the textbook model was right, New Jersey fast-food employment should fall relative to Pennsylvania's. The researchers surveyed 410 fast-food restaurants in both states before and after the increase.

The result, published in the American Economic Review in 1994: fast-food employment in New Jersey rose by about 13% relative to Pennsylvania after the minimum wage increase. Stores that had been paying below the new minimum (those most affected by the law) actually increased employment more than stores paying above $5.00. The textbook prediction was decisively wrong, not just in magnitude but in sign.

What's the explanation? Several possibilities, each with some support in subsequent research:

  • Monopsony power. Many low-wage labor markets are dominated by a few employers (fast-food chains in a town, for example). A monopsonist already pays below the worker's marginal product. Raising the minimum wage can move closer to the competitive level — increasing employment, not decreasing it.
  • Efficiency wage effects. Higher wages reduce turnover and absenteeism, which firms find profitable. So a forced increase pays for itself.
  • Lower turnover means firms keep more workers, even if total hires don't change.
  • Modest pass-through to prices. Firms raise prices a little; consumers absorb the cost; nobody loses much.

The Card-Krueger study sparked decades of follow-up research. Some critics (notably David Neumark and William Wascher in 2000) reanalyzed the data and found weaker positive employment effects but also no strong negative ones. The current consensus among labor economists, after thirty years of accumulated evidence: moderate minimum wage increases have small or even slightly positive effects on employment in most settings. Very large increases can create employment effects, but the threshold appears to be much higher than the standard textbook predicts. David Card was awarded the 2021 Nobel Prize in Economics for this and related work in labor economics, methodological work that established the "credibility revolution" in empirical economics.

The lesson for this textbook: when the simplest model predicts something dramatic, check whether real labor markets behave like the model assumes. Often they don't. The labor market is held together by frictions, relationships, and social norms that simple supply-and-demand diagrams omit entirely.

✦ Sharma's Take — Why the standard minimum wage diagram persists despite its failures

If the Card-Krueger evidence has held up for 30 years, why does every intro textbook still teach the standard "minimum wage destroys jobs" diagram first? Two reasons:

First, pedagogical clarity. The supply-and-demand model is easy to draw and easy to manipulate. Its predictions are sharp, even if often wrong. Students learn the diagram, learn how to shift the curves, and move on. Teaching the modern view requires acknowledging that labor markets are complex, evidence-based assessments are necessary, and there isn't a single clean diagram that captures everything. That's pedagogically harder.

Second, political comfort. The standard prediction aligns with a particular political worldview: government intervention in markets is bad, prices should clear, and minimum wages hurt the people they're meant to help. Acknowledging that the prediction is empirically weak undercuts that political narrative, and so the textbook treatment lives on despite the evidence.

My approach in this textbook: teach the standard model, but always tell you when the empirics disagree. On minimum wages specifically, the empirics disagree substantially with the textbook model for moderate increases. Take both seriously, weigh them, and form your own view. That's what economics literacy actually requires.

— A.S.

14.6 Job Search Theory: A Simple Model

📗 Intermediate Level — Why even rational job seekers stay unemployed for a while

Why doesn't an unemployed worker just take the first job offer they get? Because not all jobs are equally good. Wages vary; benefits vary; locations vary; commutes vary; growth potential varies. A worker who accepts the first offer may end up in a worse job than one who waited a bit longer for a better offer. Job search theory formalizes this trade-off: workers compare the expected gain from continued searching against the cost of remaining unemployed.

⚙ The Reservation Wage Rule
Accept offer if w ≥ w*
where w* = reservation wage
w*
The reservation wage — the lowest wage at which the worker is just indifferent between accepting and continuing to search. Below w*, decline and keep searching. Above w*, accept.
w
The wage offered by a specific job.

What raises w* (makes workers pickier, increases unemployment duration):

  • More generous unemployment insurance (lower cost of staying unemployed)
  • Higher savings/wealth (worker can afford to wait)
  • Spousal income (household has another earner)
  • Belief that better offers will arrive soon (information about the market)
  • Higher skill (worker expects to be matched to a higher-paying job)

What lowers w* (makes workers less picky, shortens unemployment):

  • UI benefits expiring or reduced
  • Savings running out
  • Long unemployment spells (employers discount workers who've been jobless a long time)
  • Pessimism about labor market improvement
📐 Worked Example 14.2 — UI generosity and unemployment duration
An unemployed worker receives a job offer paying $22/hour. She estimates that if she keeps searching for another 4 weeks, she has a 50% chance of receiving a better offer paying $30/hour. Her unemployment insurance pays $15/hour-equivalent. (a) What's her expected value from waiting another 4 weeks? (b) Should she accept the $22 offer? (c) If her UI is cut to $8/hour-equivalent, does her decision change?
1
(a) Expected value of waiting 4 weeks: 50% chance of $30/hour offer, 50% chance of a $22/hour offer (or similar). Expected hourly wage if she waits: 0.5 × 30 + 0.5 × 22 = $26/hour. While she waits, she earns $15/hour-equivalent (UI). The wait period earnings: $15/hour × 160 hours = $2,400. Plus the expected wage gain over 1 year if she gets the $30 job: 0.5 × ($30 − $22) × 2,000 = $8,000.
2
(b) Decision: waiting yields about $10,400 in expected gain (UI received + expected wage premium). Cost of waiting: foregone wages of $22 × 160 = $3,520. Net gain from waiting: $10,400 − $3,520 ≈ $6,880. She should keep searching.
3
(c) UI cut to $8/hour: wait-period earnings drop to $8 × 160 = $1,280. Expected wage gain unchanged at $8,000. Net gain from waiting: $9,280 − $3,520 = $5,760. Still positive — she should still wait. But the gain has shrunk substantially. For workers with worse expected offers or shorter time horizons, a UI cut would flip the decision toward accepting now.
This is the canonical insight from search theory: generous unemployment insurance lengthens unemployment spells (workers wait for better offers) but also raises the quality of matches (workers find better-fitting jobs). The U.S. has historically had less generous UI than European countries, leading to shorter unemployment durations but lower-quality matches. The 2020-2021 pandemic UI expansion ($600/week federal supplement) was the largest U.S. labor-market natural experiment in decades, raising reservation wages dramatically and contributing to the matching difficulty firms experienced in 2021-2022.

14.7 Unemployment Across Demographics and Time

📘 Principles Level — The labor market is not one market

The headline unemployment rate hides enormous variation across groups. In April 2023, when overall U-3 was 3.4%, the unemployment rate for white workers was 3.1%, for Black workers 4.7%, for Hispanic workers 4.4%, for teenagers 9.2%, and for college graduates 1.9%. These gaps reflect deep structural differences in labor market access — discrimination, geographic mismatch, network effects, and educational disparities.

Demographics also matter for understanding the trend. The U.S. unemployment rate of the 1980s averaged 7.3%; the 2010s averaged 6.2%; the 2020s so far has averaged 5.4%. Part of this decline reflects an aging workforce (older workers have lower unemployment) and a more-educated workforce (educated workers have lower unemployment). The "natural rate" we discussed in §14.2 has likely fallen 1-1.5 percentage points over the past 40 years primarily because of these demographic shifts.

U-3 unemployment rate by group, April 2023 (50-year low)
GroupRateGroupRate
Overall (all workers)3.4%By education:
Adult men3.3%Less than high school5.4%
Adult women3.1%High school grad3.9%
Teenagers (16-19)9.2%Some college2.9%
White3.1%Bachelor's degree+1.9%

Hispanic or Latino4.4%

🎮 MacroNations Connection — Unemployment in the Simulation

MacroNations tracks unemployment as a key indicator under the Stability pillar (high unemployment costs you points) and the Equality pillar (unemployment falls disproportionately on lower-skill workers, raising inequality measures). Your simulation engine starts you with a "natural rate" calibrated to your country profile — emerging economies typically have higher u* than developed ones.

Several levers affect unemployment in the simulation: monetary policy (interest rate changes affect cyclical unemployment with about a 4-quarter lag), government spending (direct demand effects), education and infrastructure (raise productivity and reduce structural unemployment over time), and welfare/UI generosity (raises reservation wages, may slow recovery but improves quality of matches). The simulation does NOT punish moderate minimum wage policies the way a textbook would — reflecting the empirical reality that moderate floors have small effects on employment.

U.S. Unemployment Rate (U-3) — Monthly, Last 25 Years
Source: BLS via FRED (Series: UNRATE). The chart shows the major episodes covered in this chapter: the 2001 recession (peak 6.3%), the 2008-09 Great Recession (peak 10.0% in October 2009), the COVID-19 collapse (peak 14.8% in April 2020, the highest since 1948), the rapid recovery to 3.4% in April 2023 (a 50-year low), and the gradual rise to ~4.3-4.4% in 2025-26.
U.S. Labor Force Participation Rate — Monthly, Last 25 Years
Source: BLS via FRED (Series: CIVPART). Notice the steady decline from ~67% in 2000 to ~63% by 2015. This is the "missing workers" problem — millions of Americans dropped out of the labor force during the 2008-09 recession and never returned. The COVID-19 shock dropped LFPR to 60.2% briefly; partial recovery has stalled around 62%. This is a hidden labor-market weakness that the headline unemployment rate misses entirely.

Chapter 14 Quiz

Measurement, types of unemployment, Beveridge curve, wage stickiness, search theory
Part A — Multiple Choice (Principles)
1. The standard U.S. unemployment rate (U-3) is computed as:
A Unemployed people / total adult population
B Unemployed people / labor force × 100%
C Unemployed people / employed people
D Labor force / adult population × 100%
2. A worker who has given up searching for a job because they believe no jobs are available is classified as:
A Unemployed (U-3)
B Employed, part-time
C Not in the labor force (and captured by U-4)
D Self-employed
3. Which type of unemployment would best describe a coal miner in West Virginia whose mine has closed permanently as the industry shrinks?
A Frictional unemployment
B Structural unemployment
C Cyclical unemployment
D Hidden unemployment
4. The U.S. unemployment rate peaked at what level in April 2020 — the highest since data collection began in 1948?
A 7.5%
B 10.0%
C 14.8%
D 22.0%
Part B — Multiple Choice (Intermediate)
5. An outward shift of the Beveridge curve (up and to the right) indicates:
A The economy has entered a deep recession
B Matching efficiency between unemployed workers and job openings has worsened
C The natural rate of unemployment has fallen
D Real wages have risen
6. The Card-Krueger (1994) study found that after New Jersey raised its minimum wage in 1992, employment at fast-food restaurants in New Jersey:
A Fell by about 20% relative to Pennsylvania, confirming the standard model
B Was unchanged relative to Pennsylvania
C Actually rose by about 13% relative to Pennsylvania, contradicting the standard model
D Was impossible to measure because of survey errors
7. If unemployment insurance benefits are made more generous, search theory predicts that:
A Unemployment duration will fall and average match quality will fall
B Unemployment duration will rise and average match quality will rise
C Both will fall
D Both will be unchanged because workers behave the same regardless of UI
Part C — Numerical Problems
8. Country X has: Adult population = 200 million. Employed = 130 million. Unemployed = 8 million. (a) Compute the labor force, the unemployment rate, the labor force participation rate, and the employment-to-population ratio. (b) Suppose 10 million people who were "not in the labor force" all start searching for jobs but only half of them succeed. Recompute the three rates. (c) Comment on whether the headline unemployment rate captures the labor-market change accurately.
Model answer: (a) Labor force = 130 + 8 = 138 million. u = 8 / 138 = 5.80%. LFPR = 138 / 200 = 69.0%. E/P = 130 / 200 = 65.0%. (b) New labor force: 138 + 10 = 148 million. New employed: 130 + 5 = 135 million. New unemployed: 148 − 135 = 13 million. u = 13 / 148 = 8.78%. LFPR = 148 / 200 = 74.0%. E/P = 135 / 200 = 67.5%. (c) The headline unemployment rate ROSE (from 5.8% to 8.8%), making it look like the labor market got worse. But in fact, MORE people are employed and MORE people are participating — the economy is in better shape. The rise in u is purely a denominator effect — the labor force expanded faster than new employment. This is a real and common phenomenon in recoveries.
9. An economy starts on a stable Beveridge curve with unemployment rate 4% and vacancy rate 4%. After a structural shock that worsens matching efficiency, the curve shifts outward: at any given vacancy rate, unemployment is 1.5 percentage points higher than before. (a) If the vacancy rate stays at 4%, what is the new unemployment rate? (b) If labor demand strengthens such that the vacancy rate rises to 7%, what would unemployment be at if matching efficiency had stayed at pre-shock levels? Now that the curve has shifted out by 1.5 percentage points, what is the actual new unemployment rate? (c) Comment on what this tells you about the limits of monetary policy in the face of structural shocks.
Model answer: (a) After the shift, at v = 4%, unemployment is now 5.5%. (b) If labor demand strengthens to v = 7% on the original curve: assuming a downward-sloping curve with each percentage point of vacancy rise reducing unemployment by ~0.5, original would have been u ≈ 2.5%. After the outward shift: actual u ≈ 4.0% (2.5% + 1.5 shift). (c) The Fed can stimulate labor demand by lowering rates, which raises vacancies and moves along the curve. But the Fed CANNOT shift the curve back inward — matching efficiency is determined by structural factors (skill mismatch, geographic mismatch, demographics) that monetary policy can't fix. This is exactly the dilemma the Fed faced in 2022: they could see that vacancies were extremely high but unemployment wasn't falling proportionally. The standard monetary tools could cool demand (move down the curve) but couldn't restore matching efficiency. The 2021-22 outward shift had to dissipate on its own through worker re-sorting, demographic adjustment, and time.
Part D — Policy Scenario
Scenario: A small, formerly industrial state has unemployment of 7% (significantly above the national rate of 4%) and a falling labor force participation rate. The dominant industry — manufacturing — has been declining for 25 years. The state legislature is considering three policies: (1) double the state minimum wage from $9 to $18 per hour; (2) provide $500/week unemployment insurance with no time limit; (3) fund a $300 million worker retraining program targeted at growing service-sector occupations. Funding is limited; the state can only afford ONE of these.
10. Write a 250-word policy memo for the Governor. Reference: the three types of unemployment from §14.2, search theory, the Card-Krueger evidence on minimum wages, and the Beveridge curve framework. Make a clear recommendation.
Model answer (sketch): The state's 7% unemployment is largely structural — caused by 25 years of manufacturing decline, not by insufficient demand. Cyclical policies cannot fix structural problems.

Option 1 (minimum wage doubling): The Card-Krueger evidence supports moderate minimum wage increases having small or zero employment effects. But doubling from $9 to $18 is far beyond moderate. The threshold beyond which negative employment effects emerge appears to be around 50-70% of median local wages; $18 likely exceeds that in many parts of the state. Risk of significant employment loss. Not recommended.

Option 2 (generous unrestricted UI): Search theory predicts this raises reservation wages, lengthens unemployment durations, and improves match quality. With structural unemployment, the issue is that good matches don't exist — workers don't have the skills employers need. UI without a time limit could increase quality of matches at the margin but doesn't address the underlying mismatch. May worsen the LFPR decline as some workers exit the labor force. Partial help at best.

Option 3 (retraining): Directly targets the structural unemployment problem. Reduces skill mismatch, shifts the Beveridge curve INWARD by improving matching efficiency, raises long-run worker productivity. Empirical evidence on retraining programs is mixed but the best programs (e.g., Job Corps, sectoral training) show meaningful returns. Recommended.

Position: Fund the $300M retraining program (Option 3). It is the only intervention that addresses the structural nature of the state's unemployment problem. Monitor outcomes carefully — direct trainees toward occupations with proven openings in the state, partner with employers, evaluate placement and wage outcomes at 1, 2, and 5 years. If money frees up later, supplement with moderate UI improvements.
This is a preview — the live textbook auto-grades MCQs and stores progress.
